vi
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
The theoretical basis for calculating the eﬀect of large deformations of plane beams
is considered. When the structural deformation becomes suﬃciently large, it aﬀects
the load carrying abilities of the structure. Thus, the concept of kinematic non-
linear eﬀects apply. The non-linear eﬀects will be presented through the Lagrangian
strain measure, which couples longitudinal and transverse motion. The theory will
be developed for numerical analysis, by introducing the Lagrangian strain measure
in the Finite Element Method (FEM). Furthermore, the possibility of applying a
prestressing force in the beam element is incorporated in the constitutive equations,
and developed through the FEM for use in numerical analysis. The applied theory
is implemented in locally based FE-program BygFEM and evaluated through
simple numerical tests, where the applicability is veriﬁed.
The kinematic non-linear theory will be applied for a static calculation, where the
Izmit Bay Bridge (IBB) will serve as case. The bridge will be modeled as a discrete
plane beam model using BygFEM, and the model will be evaluated in terms of
meshing, boundary conditions, loading, assumptions and limitations. In order to
account for the deﬂections induced by the self-weight of the bridge a layout for
the prestressing of the main cable and hangers is determined. Due to the non-
linear character of the structure, an iterative prestressing algorithm is applied. A
simpliﬁed static analysis will be conducted for the bridge in order to determine
the deﬂection and stress state of the structural elements. For this analysis, the
inﬂuence line theory is applied in order to evaluate the relevance of diﬀerent load
arrangements for the variable traﬃc load. Four diﬀerent load arrangements are
considered for the analyses of the bridge. The analysis is conducted without limit
state considerations. Nevertheless, the deﬂections and stress states are determined
within an acceptable limit.
The theoretical basis for calculating the response of non-linear structures exposed to
dynamic loading is considered. As for the static case, the non-linear eﬀects will be
established through the Lagrangian strain measure, which couples the longitudinal
and transverse motion. The theory will again be developed for numerical analysis
of general systems, by introducing it in the FEM. The concept of time integration
is introduced for solving the dynamic system. The explicit CDM-method and the
implicit Newmark-method is implemented in BygFEM and evaluated through
vii
==PAGE:8==
simple numerical tests, in order to evaluate numerical errors, stability issues and
computational time. The implicit Newmark algorithm enables the use of larger
time steps and proves superior in terms of computational time.
For discretized structures that include a great number of dofs, the use of time
integration methods can prove inconvenient due to great computational time. The
advantage of selected basis reduction methods is investigated. The reduced basis
is applied using a second-order Taylor series expansion of the displacement ﬁeld.
Thus, derivatives of the linear mode shapes enter in the displacement formulation,
in order to describe the coupling of individual modes of vibration. Two diﬀerent
approaches for the basis formulation are investigated: The reduced basis method
assuming individual modal coordinates (RBIC) and the reduced basis method using
the Taylor basis to decouple the modal coordinates (RBTB). Both methods are
implemented in BygFEM and evaluated through simple numerical tests. Based
on the preliminary calculations of simple structures, a large potential in the reduced
basis methods is observed concerning a reduction in computational time.
The non-linear dynamic theory will be applied for dynamic analysis of the IBB.
As for the static case, the bridge will be modeled as a discrete plane beam model
using BygFEM. The model will be evaluated in terms of structural damping and
dynamic loading. In order to determine the vibration characteristics of the IBB,
a linear frequency analysis is conducted, and compared to experimental test for
veriﬁcation of the numerical model.
A great resemblance is observed between
the numerical calculations of the present work and the experimental tests, and the
model is accepted. The reduced basis methods are compared to the time integration
method, in terms of validity of the displacement of the considered structural model
and convergence of the response obtained from the analysis. The RBIC-method
experiences an unfavorably low convergence rate, and the RBTB-method converges
towards an incorrect solution to the response. Through an evaluation of the modal
coordinates in the applied basis, it is deduced that the second-order Taylor series
expansion of the displacement ﬁeld yields a truncation error for the considered
magnitudes of displacement. Thus, the formulation of the reduced basis in the
present form is concluded to be inapplicable for the suspension bridge analysis.
Finally, a non-linear analysis is conducted for the oscillating prestressed bridge,
in order to determine the displacement, natural frequency development and stress
state of the bridge.
A comparative study is conducted between the linear and
non-linear dynamic analysis to evaluate the signiﬁcance of the eﬀects of geometri-
cal non-linearity of the IBB. It is observed that the non-linearity of the displace-
ment increases for an increasing amplitude. Furthermore, it is observed, that the
complexity of the conﬁguration oscillation increases with the non-linearity of the
response.
A stress analysis is conducted for speciﬁc points in the structure, and the ratio of
utilization is evaluated for the considered time period, and it is concluded, that
amplitudes yielding non-linear responses of the bridge can be obtained.
viii
==PAGE:9==
The response of the side spans seems to be aﬀected greatly by the non-linearity of
the structure. Therefore, a generalized threshold for evaluation of the requirements
for non-linear analysis is deﬁned. It is observed, that the eﬀect of the geometrical
non-linearity of the structure is highly inﬂuenced by the exciting load. Conclusively,
it is assessed that the non-linearity of the structure can be disregarded for vortex
shedding, due to the low displacements observed from this phenomenon. However,
it cannot be concluded that the non-linearity of the structure is insigniﬁcant for
other exciting loads.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Suspension bridge structures represent a widely spread solution to the demands of
large crossings. The suspension bridge has the attractive properties of achieving
long spans with a minimum of material consumption. The structure has relatively
limited demands for construction, meaning that except for the installation of the
initial temporary cables, limited access from below is required during construction,
keeping the crossing clear. Furthermore, the structure has an aesthetic appearance
with curved and linear features making it an attraction to many.
However, the ﬂexibility of the structure, caused by the cable system and the long
span, makes the suspension bridge prone to excessive geometry changes.
This
introduces the need for developing a formulation which accounts for the non-linear
eﬀect related to the large geometry change. Thus, the concept of kinematic non-
linear eﬀects must be considered. Furthermore, the self-weight of the suspension
bridge induces deformation of the structure resulting in an initially sagged state. In
order to limit this sag, the main cable and hangers are prestressed, and a method for
accounting for the prestressed state of the suspension bridge must be formulated,
in order to provide with adequate bridge design.
As aforementioned the suspension bridge has a relatively ﬂexible design, making
it sensitive to dynamic loading. Thus, the structure may be prone to oscillate for
certain dynamic loads, e.g. wind loading. Consequently, securing the structure
against dynamic loads is an important and demanding issue for the design of long-
span suspension bridges.
In practical engineering, the dynamic analysis of the prestressed suspension bridges
is often carried out using a linearized dynamic analysis. Thus, the stiﬀness develop-
ment of the oscillating bridge is neglected, resulting in a simpler, but less accurate
analysis. This linearized simpliﬁcation may result in a limited evaluation of the
response.
Applying a full non-linear dynamic analysis, results in a more realistic description
of the vibrating characteristics, as the structural stiﬀness update and the coupling
of individual modes of vibration, caused by the non-linear strain measure are ac-
counted for.
1
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 1.
Introduction
Diﬀerent solution strategies exist for solving the non-linear dynamic problem, and
a widely known method is time integration. Several time integration schemes exist,
with diﬀerent properties for accuracy, stability and computational time. However,
all of these methods results in time-consuming calculations for large discrete nu-
merical non-linear models.
This introduces the need for a more computational eﬃcient modeling of kinematic
non-linear structures. In order to formulate a more eﬃcient method of calcula-
tion, the challenges surrounding the development of a non-linear modal analysis is
investigated, based on the work performed in [9].
In essence, the overall objective of the present thesis is then formulated as:
Objective of thesis:
Evaluation of computational eﬃciency in
dynamic modeling of kinematic non-linear structures,
with focus on suspension bridge structures.
In order to answer the considered objective, the thesis is divided into individual
chapters, with content as presented below:
Chapter 2 determines the theoretical basis for calculating kinematic non-linear
structures. The theory will be developed for numerical analysis, by introducing the
Lagrangian strain measure in the Finite Element Method (FEM). Furthermore, the
chapter deals with the application of a prestressing force in the plane beam element.
Chapter 3 presents a static analysis of a suspension bridge, where the Izmit Bay
Bridge (IBB) will serve as case. A layout for the prestressing of the main cable
and hangers is suggested and a simpliﬁed static analysis will be conducted for the
bridge loaded by traﬃc load, in order to determine the deﬂection and stress state
of the structural elements.
Chapter 4 determines the theoretical basis for calculating the dynamic response of
kinematic non-linear structures. The theory will again be developed for numerical
analysis of general systems, by introducing it in the FEM. Furthermore, diﬀerent
solution strategies will be evaluated in terms of computational eﬃciency.
Chapter 5 presents a dynamic analysis of IBB. The model will be evaluated in
terms of a linear frequency analysis before advancing to the non-linear computa-
tions. The diﬀerent solution strategies are evaluated for the considered structural
model. Finally, a comparative study is conducted between the linear and non-
linear dynamic analysis, in order to assess at what point the non-linear eﬀects of
the oscillation becomes signiﬁcant.
2
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 1.
Introduction
1.1
Numerical Calculations
Throughout the report, the applied theory has been implemented in a locally based
ﬁnite element program, which is appended on a USB-stick.
The locally based
program is written in Matlab and is denoted BygFEM. The program was initially
suited for performing static linear and non-linear analysis of a structure without
including prestress. Thus, the FEM-equations and framework for solving the static
problem are altered in order to account for additional eﬀects in the performed
analysis. Furthermore, the program was initially suited for performing the linear
modal analysis, and linear and non-linear explicit central diﬀerence method (CDM)
without accounting for prestressing. Thus, the FEM-equations and framework for
solving the dynamic problem are altered in order to account for additional eﬀects in
the performed analysis. The alteration includes implementation of the non-linear
Newmark algorithm and the reduced basis methods in the program. No initial
scripts for the reduced basis methods were provided, and the entire framework for
these analyses are established.
Throughout the present report, extensive continuous algorithm testing is performed
in order to evaluate the validity of the program.
A number of these tests are
documented throughout the report. Furthermore, the tests are appended on the
USB-stick. A road map of the folder structure and an overview of in which sections
the tests are performed are presented in Appendix S.
Finally, the calculation conducted and presented throughout the report is appended
on the USB-stick. Likewise, a road map of the folder structure and an overview of
in which sections the tests are performed are presented in Appendix S.
3
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 1.
Introduction
4
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2
Theoretical Basis for Non-linear
Static FE-Analysis
In the present chapter, the theoretical basis for calculating the eﬀect of large defor-
mations of plane beams is considered. When the structural deformation becomes
suﬃciently large, it aﬀects the load carrying abilities of the structure. Thus, the
concept of kinematic non-linear eﬀects apply. The non-linear eﬀects will be repre-
sented through the Lagrangian strain measure, which couples the longitudinal and
transverse motion. For structures experiencing excessive geometry changes, e.g. a
transversely loaded cable, the need for developing a formulation which accounts for
the non-linear behavior is highly relevant. The theory will be developed for numer-
ical analysis, by introducing the Lagrangian strain measure in the Finite Element
Method. Furthermore, the possibility of applying a prestressing force in the beam
element is incorporated in the constitutive equations, and developed through the
FEM for use in numerical analysis. When dealing with non-linear analysis the FE-
equations must be solved iteratively. The Newton-Raphson method is presented
as an applicable method for this. Finally, the applied theory is implemented in lo-
cally based FE-program BygFEM and evaluated through simple numerical tests,
in order to verify the applicability of the program for further analysis.
2.1
Orientation and Naming Convention of the Plane
Beam
The beam plane follows the (x,y) plane of the Cartesian coordinate system, as
depicted in Fig. 2.1.

Fig. 2.1: Orientation of plane beam in Cartesian coordinate system and
naming convention of sectional forces and moments.
The sectional forces, normal force, shear force and moment, are denoted N, V
and M, respectively. The axial deformation of the beam is denoted ux and the
deformation transverse to the beam is denoted uy. The rotation of the beam is
denoted θ, and deﬁned as the derivative of the transverse displacement duy/dx.
2.2
The Lagrange Strain Measure
A common way of introducing the kinematic non-linear eﬀects in a FE-formulation
is through the Lagrangian strain measure. In the following section, the theory of
the Lagrangian strain will be developed as presented in [1].
The Lagrangian strain measure is a non-linear strain measure that can be used
to describe the eﬀects of large displacements.
The strain measure is described
through a so-called material formulation, where a material point is described by
a given set of coordinates in an initial ﬁxed coordinate system, i.e. the deformed
state is described with reference to the undeformed state.
The generalized strains of the beam consist of the axial strain ε and the curvature
κ of the beam. To account for the eﬀect of large displacements the axial strain
is introduced as the Lagrangian strain measure, while curvature is given by the
ordinary linear strain measure i.e. κ = u′′
y. Thus, the Lagrangian strain measure
can be derived by considering a simple bar element.

2.3
Virtual Lagrange Strain
The basis for solving mechanical problems is the governing diﬀerential equations
and associated boundary conditions that models the given problem. Together, the
diﬀerential equation and boundary conditions constitute the mechanical problem
in the strong form. Utilizing calculus of variations and the divergens theorem, this
is transformed to the weak form, which for continuum mechanics is the equivalent
of the virtual work equation, [11]:
δWext = δWint
(2.7)
By obtaining the weak form of the problem, the requirements for continuity in the
solution is lowered, which enables approximating the ﬁeld variables by interpolation
functions. This makes the virtual work equation ideal as a starting point for the
development of the FEM equations.
7
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 2.
Theoretical Basis for Non-linear Static FE-Analysis
In (2.7) it is stated, that the external work due to a virtual displacement is equal
to the internal work due to a corresponding virtual strain, cf. [1].
In order to to ensure equilibrium in the virtual work equation, the virtual strains
δε are deﬁned as the additional strains arising from a imposed virtual displacement
δu:
δε = ε(u + δu) −ε(u)
(2.8)
By substitution of (2.5) into (2.8) the virtual (variational) Lagrange strain is de-
termined:

2.4
FEM Implementation
As discussed in section 2.3, the virtual work equation can be utilized for lowering
the requirements for continuity in the solution to a given mechanical problem,
thereby allowing approximation of the ﬁeld variables by interpolation. Thus, a
continuous structural system can be subdivided into elements and described as a
discrete system.
The displacement ﬁeld u of a given element can be interpolated by a linear com-
bination of the nodal displacements/rotations vl and the nodal displacement in-
terpolation functions Ni collected in the displacement interpolation matrix N, cf.
[12]:
u = N vl
(2.13)
8
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 2.
Theoretical Basis for Non-linear Static FE-Analysis
The displacement ﬁeld is developed for the Lagrange beam element. The element is
based on a standard beam element with six degrees of freedom (dofs), as depicted
in Fig. 2.3.

The axial Lagrange strain measure ε, in the generalized strain (2.18), consist of
two terms. The ﬁrst term, Bεvl is a polynomial of the ﬁrst order, as Bε is the ﬁrst
row of the strain interpolation matrix B. The second term, 1/2 (vl)T Gvl, however,
is a polynomial of the fourth order. This mismatch in polynomial degree can result
in locking of the element, cf. [11]. Thus, the implementation of an average G-
matrix, can improve convergence of the calculation, by eliminating the mismatch
in polynomial degree in the axial Lagrange strain measure. This is presented in
Appendix C.

2.7

Until now, the theory has been described with respect to the individual element.
Therefore, it is sought to apply this theory on a global scale.
In order to establish the global formulation all elements must be described with
reference to a global coordinate system. This is achieved by utilizing the coordi-
nate transformation matrix A, cf. [12]. The transformation applies for both the
displacement vector, nodal force vector, load vector and stiﬀness matrix:

2.8
The Newton-Raphson Method
In the present section, the Newton-Raphson method is described in accordance with
[1]. The Newton-Raphson method can be used as a method for solving problems
of non-linear character, as the case is for the non-linear FE-equation presented in
(2.44). The concept is presented for a 1-dof system.
The Newton-Raphson method is a load controlled method in which equilibrium
iterations are utilized to counteract the drift away from the solution.
The load is applied in steps of magnitude ∆P. For each load step, a state of equi-
librium between the applied external load and internal forces must be reached. As
the load-deformation path is of non-linear character, and it is approximated using
the tangential stiﬀness represented by a linear relation, the state of equilibrium
will be not be represented by the ﬁrst approximation, i.e. the applied external load
and the internal forces will be diﬀerent.
15
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 2.
Theoretical Basis for Non-linear Static FE-Analysis
Fig. 2.4: Iterative procedure in the Newton-Raphson method. Figure from
[1].
This diﬀerence is referred to as the residual load:
r = Pi −Q(ui)
(2.45)
If the value of the residual is below a given tolerance, the point is accepted, and
the next load step is applied. If the residual is not acceptable, the structure is
loaded with the residual to produce a further increment in the displacement. The
residual is recalculated and checked with the tolerance again. This procedure is
repeated until a state of equilibrium is reached. The Newton-Raphson algorithm
is presented schematically in Algorithm 1, cf. [1].
Algorithm 1 Newton-Raphson
1: ∆P = load increment
2: u1 = 0, P1 = 0
3: for i = 2 to nstep + 1 do % load stepping
4:
Pi = Pi+1 + ∆P
5:
ui = ui−1
6:
r = ∆P
7:
j = 0
8:
while |r| > ϵtol|∆P| and j < nit do % equilibrium iterations
9:
Ki = K(ui)
10:
du = K−1
i
r
11:
ui = ui + du
12:
r = Pi −Q(ui)
13:
j = j + 1
14:
end while
15: end for
16
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 2.
Theoretical Basis for Non-linear Static FE-Analysis
2.9
Testing of Algorithm
In the following sections, the individual components used in the Newton-Raphson
(NR) algorithm in the locally based FE-program BygFEM is tested. The tests are
performed to ensure, that the algorithm provides accurate results after alteration
of the numerical code and the implementation of the prestress.
The NR-algorithm is rather stable, and equilibrium can be obtained even with an
incorrect tangential stiﬀness if enough equilibrium iterations are applied. Therefore
it is necessary to perform individual tests of both the tangent stiﬀness matrix
KT (V), and the nodal force vector Q(V).
Apart from KT (V) and Q(V), the implementation of the prestressing force Pi is
tested, to assess whether prestressing of the elements results in the desired behavior.
2.9.1
Tangential Stiﬀness Matrix
In the following section it is checked, if the established tangential stiﬀness matrix
KT (V) is correct. Furthermore it is checked, if KT (V) is implemented correctly
in the in BygFEM.
As an initial check of the tangential stiﬀness matrix, a single beam element is
considered. The beam element is subjected to both rigid body- translation and
rotation to check whether forces arise in the element. The tests that are performed
on the single beam element are presented in Appendix D.
Conclusively, the results of the test are in compliance with the theory, as no forces
arise from the rigid body translation. For the rigid body rotation, forces arise in
the element due to the approximations in the Lagrangian strain measure. However,
these forces are of insigniﬁcant magnitude.
Subsequently, to check if the tangential stiﬀness matrix is correct, and if it is
implemented correctly in BygFEM, a problem with a known solution is considered.
As aforementioned, the NR algorithm is rather robust, and a converged solution can
be reached, using an incorrect tangential stiﬀness matrix, due to the equilibrium
iterations performed in the algorithm. Thus, the tangential stiﬀness matrix must
be tested without utilizing equilibrium iterations in the NR algorithm. This is done
by increasing the tolerance ϵtol and decreasing the load increment ∆P presented
in Algorithm 1.
The problem considered for this test, is the angle beam presented in Fig. 2.5, with
the non-dimensional parameters h/b = 0.024 and L2
0A/I = 3.0 · 104. According to
[2], an approximate analytical solution to the problem has been obtained in [14],
while a more direct solution has been obtained in [15].

Furthermore it is checked, if Q(V) is implemented correctly in the
BygFEM.
Similarly to the initial check performed for the tangential stiﬀness matrix, a single
beam element is considered. The beam element is subjected to both rigid body-
translation and rotation to check if the local displacement ﬁelds give rise to any
nodal forces. The tests that are performed on the single beam element are presented
in Appendix D.
Conclusively, the results of the test are in compliance with the theory, as no nodal
forces arise from the rigid body translation and insigniﬁcant forces arises from the
rigid body rotation.

2.9.3

In the following section, it is investigated if the implementation of the prestressing
force Pi in the element formulation, yields a correct response of the element.
The prestressing is implemented in the element both to counteract the external
forces, and to increase the stiﬀness of the structure axially and transversely. There-
fore several checks are performed. The checks are conducted on a single element
cantilevered beam model, as depicted in Fig.
2.8.

Checking the prestress implementation in the elements with the non-linear La-
grangian strain is somewhat cumbersome, as the properties of the element changes
with the deﬂection. Therefore, as depicted in Fig. 2.8, six diﬀerent load scenarios
has been considered, to evaluate the prestress.
The purpose of the load scenarios are; (A) to establish an initial case for compar-
ison to an axial external load F, (B) to check if the prestressing force result in
axial strains in the element, (C) to check whether a prestressing force of the same
magnitude as an axial external load, will result in zero axial strains in the element,
(D) to check if the axial stiﬀness of the element is increased when it is in a state of
prestress, and additional external load is applied to the element, (E) to establish
an initial case for comparison to an external transverse load Fy, (F) to check if the
transverse stiﬀness of the element is increased when it is in a state of prestress,
and an external transverse load is applied to the element.
The element strain for the individual cases are presented in table 2.1:

Considering the strain for the element in case (B) it is noticed, that the contraction
of the element is of greater magnitude than the elongation of the element in case
(A). This can be explained by considering the deﬁnition of the Lagrangian strain
measure (2.18). When the element is in a state of tension both terms in the strain
measure is positive, whereas only the second order term remains positive, when
the element is compressed, as the case is when prestressing is introduced.
Considering case (C) it is apparent, that the contribution from the prestressing
force counteracts the contribution from the external load, as an axial displacement,
ux, of zero is observed.
Comparing case (A) and (D), where an unbalanced external axial load is introduced
in both cases, it is noticed, that case (D), which is in a prestressed state, yields
a smaller axial displacement, than case (A). Thus the stiﬀness increases, which
is a result of the prestress implementation in the tangential stiﬀness and matrix,
section 2.6, and the nodal force vector, section 2.5.
Finally, case (E) and (F) is compared, to investigate if the prestressing the element
inﬂuences the transverse stiﬀness. In both cases the cantilevered beam is loaded by
the transverse force Fy, however, in case (F) the element is in a state of prestress.
Considering the vertical displacement uy, which is presented in table 2.1 a signif-
icant decrease is observed for the prestressed state. Thus, the transverse stiﬀness
increases which is in compliance with the theory presented in section 2.5 and 2.6.
Conclusively, the prestress is considered to be implemented correctly.

Chapter 3
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay
Bridge
In the present chapter, a static analysis of a suspension bridge will be conducted.
Initially, a brief discussion of the articulation of suspension bridges, in general,
will be conducted. For the structural analysis, the IBB will serve as case. The
bridge will be modeled as a discrete plane beam model using the locally based
FE-program BygFEM, and the model will be evaluated in terms of meshing,
boundary conditions, loading, assumptions and limitations. In order to account for
the deﬂections induced by the self-weight of the bridge a layout for the prestressing
of the main cable and hangers is suggested. Due to the non-linear character of the
structure, an iterative prestressing algorithm is applied. Finally, a simpliﬁed static
analysis will be conducted for the bridge in order to determine the deﬂection and
stress state of the structural elements. For this analysis, the bridge will be loaded
by a variable traﬃc load, and the inﬂuence line theory is applied in order to evaluate
the relevance of the diﬀerent load arrangements.
3.1
Articulation of Suspension Bridge
In the present section, the individual structural members in a suspension bridge
will be described brieﬂy. The central structural components are illustrated in Fig.
3.1. The section will primarily serve as a description of the static behavior of the
individual members, and the boundary conditions that are associated with them.
23
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
Fig. 3.1: Individual structural components in a suspension bridge.
Illustration from [3].
Bridge girder:
The bridge girder is a longitudinal beam that distributes the load from e.g. traﬃc
to the hangers. For large suspension bridges, the girder is typically constructed
as a box proﬁle in steel. Considering the connection between the girder and the
pylons or abutments, diﬀerent support arrangements can be applied. Thus, a static
model suitable for the present case can be established by introducing e.g. torsional
constraints, expansion joints etc. Diﬀerent solutions for diﬀerent bridge girders has
been investigated further in [6] and [16].
Hangers: The hangers act as local intermediate vertical supports along the bridge
girder. The load coming from the girder is transferred as tension in the hangers to
the main cable, where the hangers deliver a point load.
Main cable: The forces from the hangers are transferred through the main cable
in tension to the pylon tops and the anchorage. In order to do this eﬃciently, the
cable is highly dependent on the sag to span ration, which is usually between 1/11
and 1/9 of the length, cf. [6].
The geometrically non-linear character of the cable is particularly pronounced by
the geometrical changes in the cable that follow from diﬀerent load conﬁgurations.
If uniformly loaded, the cable follows a parabolic curve, as depicted in Fig. 3.2(a),
while a cable loaded by self-weight alone would follow a hyperbolic curve as depicted
in Fig. 3.2(b), cf. [17].
24
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
L
f
p
R
R
H
H
R
R
x
p
(a)
L
f
p
R
R
H
H
R
R
x
p
(b)
Fig. 3.2: Equivalent beam section resulting in a (a) parabolic cable curve or
(b) hyperbolic cable curve.
As the load from the hangers attacks in a point,the main cable would not follow
an entirely smooth curve, but rather comprise of linear parts between the hangers,
where the cable is kinked.
Pylon: The pylons act as global intermediate vertical supports to the main cable.
The load from the main cable is introduced in the pylon as a vertical force com-
ponent, that is transferred to the foundation as axial compression in the pylon.
If horizontal equilibrium is not secured in the pylon top, the horizontal force is
obtained as a moment. Dependent on the case diﬀerent types of pylons can be
preferred e.g. ﬂexible pylons, rigid pylons, etc. This is elaborated in [6] and [16].
In addition to supporting the main cables, the pylons can act as supports for
the bridge girder when it is subjected to a transverse load. This behavior is not
considered further in the present work.
Anchor block: In order to obtain the horizontal stability at the pylon tops, the
main cable must be anchored. The type of anchorage can be divided into to main
groups; 1) External anchorage, where the main cable deliverers the tension force to
an anchor block and 2) Self-contained anchorage, where the main cable transmits
the force to the bridge girder, where it is obtained in compression.
3.2
Geometry and Sectional Properties of IBB
In the present section, the geometry of the IBB will be presented brieﬂy. Further-
more, the sectional properties used for further analysis will be presented.
The main span of the IBB is 1550 m and each of the side spans are 566 m. The
span is carried by a system of hangers and main cables, that is suspended from
25
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
two steel pylons of an approximate height 240 m. The overall bridge geometry is
presented in the elevation in Fig. 3.3, and can be found in Appendix A as well.
When considering the part of the bridge that is located between the abutments,
the bridge is symmetric about the center line. Thus, the south side span, that is
depicted in Fig. 3.4, is used for describing the support conditions.
Fig. 3.3: Elevation view of geometry of the IBB. Illustration from [4].
Fig. 3.4: Elevation view of southern part of IBB. Illustration from [4].
In Fig. 3.4 the foundation for the pylon is illustrated. The foundation consists of
a concrete column resting on a slab. The slab is supported by a pile group that
is embedded in the ground. At the end of the side spans, where the abutments
and side spans are connected, expansion joints are introduced in order to allow
longitudinal movement of the bridge deck girder arising from e.g.
temperature
changes. The expansion joints are denoted EJ in the drawing material, Appendix
A.
The bridge deck girder is constructed as a closed steel box girder in segments of
25 m. The cross-section has an approximate height of 4.75 m and an approximate
width of 36 m, as presented in Fig. 3.5. The bridge girder section is symmetric
about the center line, and each side carries 3 traﬃc lanes - 1 for heavy traﬃc closest
to the hangers and 2 for normal traﬃc. Furthermore, an inspection lane is located
26
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
on the outside of the hangers, between the crash barrier and pedestrian railing, as
depicted in Fig. 3.5. The girder geometry varies along the longitudinal direction
of the bridge, resulting in a varying stiﬀness of the deck girder. The parameters
for the varying cross section are presented in detail in Appendix E.
Fig. 3.5: Bridge girder cross section. Illustration from [4].
The bridge deck girder is supported by vertical hangers made from parallel-wire
strands with varying dimensions, as presented in Appendix A. In the present case,
all hangers are modeled using the same cross-section.
The hangers are connected to the main cable, which is composed of 110 strands
each of 127 high strength steel wires with a diameter of 5.92 mm (in the back span
two additional strands are introduced). The connection between the hangers and
the main cable is depicted in Fig. 3.6. The main cable spans 1550 m between the
pylons and has a sag of approximately 172 m. This results in a sag-to-span ratio
of approximately 1/9. This is in compliance with the guideline for a beneﬁcial
sag-to-span ratio: 1/9 - 1/11, that is presented in [6].
27
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
Fig. 3.6: Hanger connections. Illustration from [4].
As the main cable is highly stressed, it delivers a great horizontal force towards
the center of the bridge at the pylon top. This force must be balanced by the cable
in the side spans, which means that the cable must be anchored as discussed in
section 3.1. In the present case, an anchor block is used for ensuring the horizontal
stability, this is illustrated in Fig. 3.4.
Considering Fig. 3.4 it should be noticed, that the cable changes direction above the
abutment yielding a kink in the cable. Thus, a compression force is introduced in
the abutment/pier. The magnitude of the compression force can vary considerably
with the change in angle in the kink. The eﬀects of this phenomenon will not be
elaborated further in the present report.
The two pylons in IBB are constructed as portal pylons with two cross beams each.
From the pylon top to the top of the concrete section there are approximately 240
m, as depicted in ﬁgure 3.7. The cross beams are constructed with a signiﬁcant
height in order to secure a ﬁxed connection between the inclined pylon legs, thus
supplying the necessary transverse stability to the pylon. The geometry of the
pylon legs vary with the height of the pylons, the varying stiﬀness is presented in
Appendix E.

For the analysis of the IBB, diﬀerent mechanical properties associated with the

The bridge deck girder and pylons vary in cross-section, yielding varying cross-

3.3
Plane Non-linear Bridge Model
The IBB is modeled as a plane beam model in using the FE-program BygFEM.
The established model is to be used for both the static- and dynamic analysis of
the bridge.
In the following section, the assumptions surrounding the modeling of the bridge as
a plane beam model is explained. Furthermore, a simpliﬁed bridge geometry suit-
30
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
able for the FE-modeling is presented. Finally, the discretization of the structural
members represented in the bridge is discussed.
3.3.1
Topology of Bridge Model
Based on the geometry of the Izmit Bay Bridge presented in section 3.2, a plane
beam model is established. In the present case, it is sought to model the bridge
geometry as it is expected to be, in the case where self-weight and prestress act on
the bridge. The geometry of the bridge is sketched in Fig. 3.9.
Fig. 3.9: Sketch of geometry of Izmit Bay Bridge and its boundary
conditions in a side view. Illustration from [3].
As depicted in Fig. 3.9 the anchor blocks are disregarded in the model, meaning
that the cable supports are located at the abutment pier, at the point of the bridge
deck support. This slightly changes the geometry of the main cable at the side
spans, but it is assumed, that it will not aﬀect the results signiﬁcantly. It is noted,
that the cable and the bridge deck does not interact at this point, and the structural
elements should not share nodes. Furthermore, as depicted in Fig. 3.9, the pylon
foundations are disregarded as structural components, and only the steel part of
the pylons (section C and D from Fig. 3.8) are modeled.
As mentioned in section 3.1, cables follow a parabolic curve when subjected to
uniformly distributed load. The structural and superimposed dead load, which
governs the conﬁguration of the cable for the bridge, is represented as an approx-
imately uniformly distributed load along the length of the bridge. Furthermore,
according to [6], the self-weight of a cable can be approximated reasonably as a
uniformly distributed load for cables with small sag to span ratios. Thus, the main
cable can be described by parabolic curves.
The parabolic shape of the cable in the main span can be described by using three
known points in the cable; One at each pylon top and at one at the center line
of the main span, Fig.
3.9.
For the side spans, however, only two points are
known. According to [6], the cable curve in the side spans has the same curvature
at the pylon top, as the cable curve in the main span. Thus, the parabolic can be
described using the point at the top of the pylon, the point at the anchorage of
31
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
the cable, and the curvature of the cable. The second-order polynomial equation
describing the main cable is presented in Appendix F.
The hangers are modeled as vertical structural members.
The position of the
hangers is determined based on the drawing material, and in general, a distance of
25 m is used between the hangers. Around the pylons, the distance between the
hangers deviates from the usual 25 meters, as depicted in Fig. 3.10.
Fig. 3.10: Hanger placement. Only a single half is depicted, as the bridge
is symmetric around the centerline. Illustration from [4].
The bridge deck is assumed to follow a smooth curve along the length of the
bridge. Considering the elevation of the bridge deck at diﬀerent points, Fig. 3.3,
it is noticed, that the curve must include ﬁve points; one at each abutment, one at
each pylon and one at mid-span. Thus, the curve for the bridge deck is described
by a fourth order polynomial, as presented in Appendix F.
The pylons are modeled as vertical structural members, as depicted in Fig. 3.9.
As aforementioned, the pylon foundations are not included in the model.
3.3.2
Assumptions and Limitations
By analyzing the bridge using a plane beam model, some limitations have to be
taken into account.
First of all the bridge deck is modeled as a plane beam,
therefore eﬀects from transverse loading e.g. wind perpendicular to the direction
of the span cannot be analyzed. Furthermore, the bridge is not assessed for eﬀects
coming from asymmetric loading on the deck, i.e. the torsional behavior of the
bridge deck girder is not accounted for.
When using the plane beam model, the bridge is considered globally. Therefore,
local eﬀects e.g. local instability phenomena and fatigue are neglected in the model.
32
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
The local eﬀects include the behavior of the connections as well.
The main cable is supported by the anchor blocks, which are rather rigid supports.
Sliding can occur in the anchor blocks, but it is disregarded in the model and the
cable supports are modeled as inﬁnitely stiﬀ. Apart from the anchor blocks, the
main cable is supported by the pylons as well. The connection between the main
cable and the pylon would be constructed using a saddle. The friction between
the saddle and the cable would be of great magnitude preventing sliding of the
cable. Thus, the connection between the pylon and the cable is modeled without
any relative motion, i.e. the pylon and the cable are sharing the same node.
The connection between the hangers and the main cable and the hangers and the
bridge deck allows the hangers to rotate relative to the main cable and the deck.
The connections are depicted in Fig. 3.6. In the beam model, the connections
are modeled as bending stiﬀ, as no hinges are introduced. This is assumed to be
acceptable due to the small bending inertia of the hangers. Thus, the hanger will
act primarily axially, and no signiﬁcant moment will arise.
The bridge deck is supported by hangers as explained previously. At the end of
the side spans, the deck is supported vertically by the abutment piers. At the
abutments, a thermal expansion joint allows longitudinal movement allowing the
deck to contract and elongate when it experiences changes in temperature. In the
beam model, the abutment pier is assumed to be inﬁnitely stiﬀin the vertical
direction. Furthermore, the connections are modeled without taking the friction
into account, i.e. allowing the deck to move freely in the horizontal direction. At
the pylons, no interaction between the bridge deck girder and pylon is taken into
account, as the bridge deck can move freely both horizontally and vertically at the
pylon.
The pylons are supported by great concrete foundations. In the plane beam model,
the foundations are assumed to be inﬁnitely stiﬀand have suﬃcient structural
capacity. Thus, the pylons are ﬁxed against both rotation and translation vertically
and horizontally at the footings. Potential sliding mechanisms in the foundation
are thereby not accounted for.
3.3.3
Elements and Meshing
The bridge is modeled using non-linear plane beam elements in which the La-
grangian strain measure is implemented along with the possibility of introducing a
prestressed state, see Chapter 2. Applying the non-linear elements are utmost im-
portant in order to describe the geometric non-linearity in the transversely loaded
main cable, but nevertheless, the element is applied to all structural components as
the calculation is of non-linear character. In order to limit the computational cost,
the discretization of each structural components are evaluated and the element
number is minimized to a quantity still providing adequate results.
33
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
The main cable primarily acts in tension, and as no load is imposed on the cable
between the hangers, apart from the self-weight of the cable, the strain of the
main cable segment between the hangers is assumed to be constant.
Thus, a
single element between the hangers provides a reasonable approximation of the
displacement ﬁeld, and the main cable is discretized with a node at each hanger
position.
Similarly to the main cable segments between the hanger attachments, the hangers
are not subjected to additional load over their length, apart from self-weight. Thus,
a single element is assessed to be suﬃcient to describe the displacement ﬁeld for
each hanger, and the hangers a discretized using a single element each.
The bridge deck primarily acts in bending, and the deformation can be described by
the normal linear curvature resulting in a 4th order displacement ﬁeld for uniform
load, cf. [10]. As a single element is limited to describing a 3rd order displacement
ﬁeld, the deck is described with a minimum of 2 elements for each deck segment be-
tween the hangers, thus achieving a reasonable approximation for the displacement
ﬁeld.
The pylons act in both compression and bending. The axial- and bending stiﬀness
of the pylon is rather great. Thus, the displacement ﬁeld for the bending of the
pylon is governing for the discretization. According to [10], the displacement ﬁeld of
the pylon would be of the 3rd order if constant bending stiﬀness and linear behavior
are assumed. However, the bending stiﬀness is not constant, and the displacement
ﬁeld is of a higher order. In the present case, the pylon is discretized using 10
elements, which should lead to a reasonable approximation of the displacement
ﬁeld.
3.4
Prestress
The following section serves as a presentation of the concept of how the prestressed
state of the IBB is obtained. The implementation of the prestressing force in the
FE-program BygFEM was discussed in Chapter 2. The following section deals
mainly with explaining the iterative procedure used for determining the prestress-
ing force for the individual elements in the IBB, here under describing the algorithm
used for the purpose.
Prestressing forces are applied in both the main cable and the hangers in order to
prevent the bridge from sagging when it is subjected to self-weight alone. Due to
the non-linear behavior of the suspension bridge, the prestressing forces must be
determined iteratively.
As described in Chapter 2, the prestressing force Pi is implemented as a stiﬀness
term in the tangential stiﬀness and as forces in the nodal force vector and load
vector. Consequently, the prestress enters the overall equilibrium and increases the
34
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
stiﬀness of the structure, thereby aﬀecting the response of the bridge in all other
load situations. Thus, it is important to know the prestressed state of the bridge
before conducting further analysis.
3.4.1
Prestress Algorithm
In order to eﬃciently determine, the prestress in each member in the IBB an
algorithm has been established. In the following section, the algorithm is presented
step by step, in order to explain the procedure for the prestressing of the bridge.
The idea of the prestressing algorithm is to introduce a prestressing force Pi in
each element in order to balance the external permanent load g coming from the
self-weight. The concept is sketched for a simple cable in Fig. 3.11.
ui

ui + ug

Fig. 3.11: Concept of prestressing.
As depicted in Fig.
3.11, the permanent load g will cause a downward global
deﬂection of the cable, which is collected in the vector ug. The prestressing force
Pi introduced axially in each element in the cable, will cause an upward global
deﬂection of the cable ui, due to the curvature of the cable, that results in a
vertical component of Pi. The magnitude of the prestressing force is then increased
iteratively until no deﬂection of the cable occurs.
The steps in the prestressing algorithm will now be explained. As the behavior of
the bridge is of non-linear character the algorithm is iterative and j denotes the
iteration number.
1. The bridge is loaded by the permanent load g and an initial prestressing
force Pj. In the ﬁrst iteration an initial prestress of P 0 is applied on each
of the e prestressed elements. The situation is analyzed in BygFEM, and

3.4.2
Prestressing of the IBB
The non-linearity of the structural members in the bridge causes the prestressing
algorithm to be rather unstable. Therefore, the determination of the prestressing
forces in the bridge has been divided into diﬀerent segments.
Initially, a preliminary prestress has been determined based on analytical theory.
Thereafter a numerical iterative calculation has been performed solely on the main
cable, disregarding the prestress in the hangers. Finally, the iterative analysis has
been used on the entire bridge. The division of the problem has been introduced
in order to limit the computational cost, as well as provide with some redundancy
in the algorithm.
Preliminary Prestressing
The algorithms are initiated by estimating a value for the prestressing force P 0.
In order to estimate a reasonable value for P 0, analytical solutions to the problem
are introduced.
37
==PAGE:60==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
The theoretical basis for the static analysis of the cable is developed by considering
the cable as an equivalent beam model, as depicted in Fig. 3.12, cf. [18]. For de-
termination of the preliminary prestressing force, only the main span is considered.

Fig. 3.12: Sectional forces in cable determined from equivalent beam model.
Assuming, that all loads are uniformly distributed along length of the bridge, and
that no horizontal movement in the top of the pylons occur, the normal forces in
the main cable can be estimated rather simply. Due to horizontal equilibrium in
the system, the horizontal force H(x) must be constant across the length of the
bridge, i.e H(x) = H.
The horizontal force is determined at mid span L/2, by simple moment considera-
tion. Thus, the relation determined from the equivalent beam model subjected to
a uniformly distributed load follows:
f (L/2) H = 1

The vertical force is determined as the shear force in the equivalent beam by
considering vertical equilibrium. The point of maximum shear in the equivalent
beam is in the support, therefore V (0) and V (L) are determined as:
V (0) = V (L) = Rv = 1

==PAGE:61==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
Knowing the desired sag of the cable, the length of the equivalent beam and the
self-weight of the structure, a preliminary prestressing force of P 0 = 410 MN is
determined from (3.8). The calculations are presented in Appendix G.
Prestressing of Main Cable
As presented in (3.10), the normal force changes throughout the main cable in the
bridge. However, only a constant initial prestressing force has been determined
from the analytical approach. In order to reach a more adequate estimate of how
the prestressing varies across the length of the bridge while keeping the computa-
tional costs at a minimum, a model is established of the main cable alone. The
cable is subjected to a load equivalent to the load of the full bridge and prestressed
by the initial prestressing force P 0 determined in the previous section.
The updated prestressing force is determined by utilizing the algorithm presented
in section 3.4.1. As described in step (2) in section 3.4.1 each node includes three
dofs, horizontal translation, vertical translation and rotation. The prestress can
be obtained by considering either of these dofs. In the present case, the prestress
is governed by the horizontal displacement of the nodes, in order to keep the hor-
izontal alignment of the main cable. Furthermore, the prestress in the elements
adjacent to the nodes in the center of the cable, is governed by the vertical dis-
placement of the node. Thus, the horizontal alignment of the main cable is kept,
while it can deﬂect vertically to ﬁt the conﬁguration caused by the load case.
Prestressing of Full Bridge
For the prestressing of the full bridge, the algorithm presented in section 3.4.1 is
used once again. For this analysis, the initial prestress for each element P 0
i , that
were determined from the prestressing of the cable, are used. Furthermore, the
bridge is loaded by self-weight. For the full bridge, not only the main cable must
be prestressed, but also the vertical hangers in the bridge. Where the prestress in
the main cable was governed primarily by the horizontal displacement of the cable,
the prestress in the hangers are governed solely by the vertical displacements of
the nodes in the bridge deck girder. This is chosen in order to keep the bridge
deck girder aligned. Consequently, a vertical displacement of the main cable can
be allowed, while the prestress of the hangers ensures that the bridge deck girder
is kept horizontal.
Establishing a prestressing algorithm that is both stable and converges towards
reasonable solutions is rather cumbersome. Due to the low compression stability
of the cable and hangers, the prestress algorithm can cause the results to diverge
from the desired solution. This can be explained by the non-linear character of the
bridge, as the non-linearity can cause an overestimation of the prestressing force
necessary for balancing the external load. This is due to the assumption regarding
linearity of the structure, that is made for determining the load scale factor λj.
Therefore an “inertia term” is introduced in the algorithm, where the load scaling
39
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
vector λj is scaled down by an additional factor αλ to reduce the sudden changes
in prestressing force. This results in a slower, but more redundant algorithm.
Furthermore, the prestressing of the of the bridge is determined by considering the
prestressing of the main cable and the hangers separately. Thus, the main cable
is prestressed until the criteria for displacement of the nodes are fulﬁlled. This
procedure is followed by prestressing the hangers until the criterion for displacement
of the bridge deck girder is fulﬁlled.
The two procedures are repeated until a
satisfying prestressed state is attained for the structure.
3.5
Static Loading
In the following sections, the static loads acting on the structure will be presented.
The static loads will comprise of the self-weight of the structure, the prestress
and the variable traﬃc load. The loads determined in the following sections will
be based on the Eurocode [5]. However, the code will not be followed directly,
and national annexes will not be taken into consideration. Furthermore, the safety
factors associated with the limit states will not be considered. Critical load arrange-
ments are presented in Fig. 3.15, based on the inﬂuence line theory, presented in
Appendix I.
3.5.1
Permanent Loading
The permanent loads acting on the bridge are; the structural dead load, the super-
imposed dead load and the prestressing forces.
As formerly discussed, the bridge is considered as a plane beam model. Therefore,
the loads are expressed as line loads across the length of the bridge. The structural
dead load is determined from parameters presented in table 3.1 and 3.2.
The

3.5.2
Variable Loading
The only variable load that is considered for the static analysis, is the traﬃc load.
Various models for traﬃc loads can be found in the Eurocode [5]. For the present
case, the traﬃc load on the bridge is determined in accordance with load model 1
(LM1). The load model consists of two partial systems; uniformly distributed load,
the UDL-system, and double axle concentrated loads, the tandem system (TS).
The UDL-system resembles the load from traﬃc distributed across the length of
the bridge, while the tandem system resembles the concentrated load coming from
axles of heavy vehicles aligned at a point along the length of the bridge.
The
loads from the tandem system can be modeled as a set of point loads separated
by a distance of 1.2 m in the longitudinal direction of the bridge. According to the
Eurocode, the two axles can be represented as one double axle for bridge spans
longer than 10 m. The tandem system is applied to each of the notional lanes, as
depicted in ﬁgure 3.13.
1.2 m
Fig. 3.13: Application of load model 1, cf. [5].
According to LM1, the UDL-system is applied to each of the separate lanes. The
magnitude of the loads is arranged according to the notional lanes. Notional lane 1,
where heavy traﬃc is applied, is typically the lane closest to the edge of the bridge.
The magnitude of both the tandem system and the UDL-system is presented in
table 3.4.

3.5.3
Load Arrangement
The traﬃc load is a variable load. Thus, the conﬁguration of the UDL and TS load
can lead to diﬀerent critical situations for the sectional forces in the bridge. In
order to assess which of the possible load conﬁgurations are critical, the inﬂuence
line theory is applied.
42
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 3.
Static Analysis of Izmit Bay Bridge
According to [8], the theory for inﬂuence lines is developed from the Maxwell-Betti
law of reciprocal deﬂection. By introducing a displacement in a point s, the eﬀect
of a load in each point i in the structure, can be evaluated by considering the
global displacement ﬁeld forced by the discontinuity displacement in s. Thus, the
most eﬀective load conﬁguration related to a certain displacement/section force of
a structure can be determined by utilizing the inﬂuence line theory. It should be
noted, that an assumption for this theory is a linear elastic structure and small
displacements, which is in conﬂict with the suspension bridge. Nevertheless, the
inﬂuence lines are utilized for determining the critical load situations for the IBB.
The inﬂuence line theory has been utilized in order to determine four critical load
scenarios of variable loading in Appendix I. Each of the scenarios are denoted as a
load case (LC). The critical LC’s are depicted in Fig 3.15.
CL

load case 03, positive moment - LC-2.3.2.
The load scenarios are chosen in order to determine the maximum normal force
and moment at speciﬁc locations in the bridge. The load case and considered eﬀect
is presented in the following list:
• LC-2.1: Maximum normal force in main cable
• LC-2.2: Maximum moment in girder at mid-span
• LC-2.3.1: Maximum negative moment in girder at pylon
• LC-2.3.2: Maximum positive moment in girder at pylon

Initially, a non-linear static analysis is conducted for the bridge, where it is sub-

the vertical deformation of the bridge deck girder is less than 5 cm, resulting in a
reasonable conﬁguration of the bridge deck in the considered state, as depicted in
Fig 3.16.

the prestressing algorithm could be to not only consider a single dof for each node
but instead consider more dofs.
This could be done either by introducing the
possibility of introducing 2 or more dofs per node in the deformation vector or by
calculating the translation in space by vector calculus or Pythagoras, instead of
only in x- and y-directions.

The moment curve can be divided into local and global behavior. Globally the
suspension bridge acts approximately similarly to a continuous beam spanned over
multiple supports, where the abutments and pylons act as supports. This is espe-
cially pronounced in LC-2.1, Fig. K.5, with limited geometry change. As a result of
the present load case, Fig. 3.15(c), a negative peak moment occurs at the hangers
adjacent to the northern pylon (At the left hand side of Fig. 3.21). This explains
why the bridge deck girder is designed with a greater moment of inertia in zone B.
Locally the moment varies parabolically as continuous beam spanned across the
hangers, as the hanger "pulls" the moment curve, causing local negative moment
in the girder.

==PAGE:73==
Chapter 4
Theoretical Basis for Linear and
Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
In the present chapter, the theoretical basis for calculating the response of struc-
tures of non-linear character exposed to dynamic loading is considered.
As for
the static case, Chapter 2, the non-linear eﬀects will be represented through the
Lagrangian strain measure, which couples the longitudinal and transverse motion.
The theory will once again be developed for numerical analysis, by introducing it
in the FEM, and the concept of time integration will be introduced as a method
for solving non-linear dynamic problems. Diﬀerent time integration schemes exist,
and in the present chapter, both an explicit and implicit time integration method
will be evaluated in terms of accuracy, stability and computational time.
For comprehensive numerical non-linear models, the time integration methods
might result in time-consuming calculations. Thus, an eﬃcient approach is rele-
vant for analyzing more comprehensive non-linear models. For non-linear systems,
the individual modes of vibration can couple due to the coupled non-linear strain
measure. In the present work, a method for conducting modal analysis able of
taking into account the coupled eﬀects will be presented. The formulation is based
on a reduced basis which is derived from a Taylor series expansion of the displace-
ment ﬁeld. Thus, derivatives of the linear mode shapes enter the displacement
formulation, enabling the description of the coupling of the individual modes. Two
methods for establishing the reduced basis are investigated.
As progress is made, the applied theory is implemented in the locally based FE-
program BygFEM and evaluated through simple numerical tests, in order to verify
the applicability of the program for further analysis. Finally, the numerical errors,
stability issues and computational time will be evaluated for a simple case.
4.1
FE-Modeling of Structural Dynamics
In the present section, the equations of motion will be derived in discretized form
by use of the principle of virtual work. The objective of this derivation is to enable
51
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
the modeling of structural problems for multi-degree-of-freedom systems using the
FEM.
As described in section 2.4, the basis for a FE-formulation of mechanical problems
can be established by the virtual work equation:
δWext = δWint
(4.1)
The external work Wext is similar to that of the static case.
It is formulated as the combination of the work performed over the domain Ω
and the boundary load t. It is remembered that the prestress term introduced an
external load term rl
i.
δW e

(4.2)
The internal work Wint is extended to include the dynamic eﬀects in the system.
Thus, besides the contribution performed by the stress σ through the virtual strains
δε, two contributions are added. The ﬁrst of the two contributions is the inertia
term, which consists of the virtual displacement δu multiplied with the inertia force,
i.e. the product of the material density ρ and acceleration ¨u. The second of the
two contributions is the damping term, which consists of the virtual displacement
δu multiplied with the damping force, i.e. the product of the damping parameter
c and velocity ˙u. The damping parameter is often chosen as viscous. The virtual
internal work can be expressed as:

simply found, cf. [1]:

4.2
Modal Analysis
According to [20], the dynamic properties of a structure can to a large extent be
described by the free vibration characteristics of the structure. For linear struc-
tures, free vibrations can be sustained at particular frequencies and mode shapes.
Furthermore, the dynamic response of the linear structure can be represented in
terms of these mode shapes.
The present section deals with explaining how the mode shapes of a structure are
identiﬁed, and how the dynamic response can be obtained from the mode shapes.
The equation of motion for the free vibration case, i.e. the case where the damping
C and the external load R are zero, is expressed as:
M ¨V + KV = 0
(4.10)
If the solution to the EOM presented in (4.10) is assumed to be harmonic, it can
be expressed as:
V(t) = ϕ sin(ωt)
(4.11)
Here ϕ is a vector representing the shape of the vibration also referred to as the
mode shape, and ω is the corresponding natural angular frequency of the system.
53
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
By substituting the response in (4.11) into the homogeneous EOM for the free
vibration in (4.10) the generalized eigenvalue problem can be obtained:
(K −ω2M)ϕ = 0
(4.12)
Nontrivial solutions to the eigenvector ϕ can only be found when the matrix (K −
ω2K) is singular, i.e. when ω2 is an eigenvalue, cf. [20].
For a discretized system of N free dofs the complete solution to the generalized
eigenvalue problem consists of N sets of eigenvalues and eigenvectors that can be
arranged in corresponding pairs of natural angular frequencies ωj and mode shape
vectors ϕj, [20]:
ωj ,
ϕj ,
j = 1, 2, . . . , N
(4.13)
The modes can conveniently be arranged in a global basis matrix Φ:
Φ = [ ϕ1 ϕ2 . . . ϕN ]
(4.14)
In [20] a set of orthogonality relations with respect to the mass M and the stiﬀness
K is obtained. It follows from these relations, that a set of diagonal matrices, m
and k, can be obtained when the mass M and the stiﬀness K are multiplied with
the global basis matrix Φ:
m = ΦT MΦ =

The computational time for the modal analysis can be reduced by realizing, that not
every mode shape must be included in the solution basis (4.11), as not every mode
shape is signiﬁcant for the overall vibration. Instead of spanning the full solution
space by considering N modes the response V can be described approximately by
spanning a reduced basis consisting of a reduced number of signiﬁcant modes alone
cf. [21].
In order to evaluate the signiﬁcance of each mode in the overall solution, the
mechanical energy of the vibration is considered. The mechanical energy of the
oscillating structural system is found as the sum of the kinetic energy and the
elastic energy, cf. [20].

vibrating system is allocated, and the full solution space basis can be reduced.
4.3
Newmark Time Integration
Diﬀerent solution strategies for the equation of motion of the multi-degree-of-
freedom-system applies. For linear systems, the dynamic problem can be reduced
to a corresponding set of uncoupled single-degree-of-freedom systems, as described
in section 4.2. This solution strategy may require extensive computation. Thus,
eﬃcient numerical time integration is a strong and important tool for determining
the solution of linear, as well as non-linear, dynamic problems, cf. [20].
In the provided FE-program BygFEM, non-linear dynamic problems can be solved
using the explicit central diﬀerence method (CDM), which is an explicit time inte-
gration scheme. This method requires rather small time steps to ensure stability,
resulting in low computational eﬃciency. Thus, the beneﬁts of introducing the
implicit non-linear Newmark time integration algorithm is evaluated.
In the following sections, the theory surrounding the non-linear Newmark time
integration is explained.
Furthermore, the implementation of the algorithm in
BygFEM and the stability and accuracy of the algorithm is checked, in order to
ensure that accurate results are obtained using this method.
4.3.1
General Concept of Time Integration
The principle of time integration is to discretize the considered continuous time
interval into a set of discrete points in time ti, and calculate the system state
vectors (V(ti), ˙V(ti)) in each of these points.
57
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
t
V (t)
ti
ti+1
∆t
Fig. 4.1: Discrete time response.
Diﬀerent solution schemes apply, and the time integration is primarily divided into
two categories; Explicit time integration schemes and implicit time integration
schemes.
For the explicit time integration the state of the system at time ti+1 is evaluated
based on the state of the system in the present and the previous time steps (ti,
ti−1, . . . ) and the loading over the interval of ti to ti+1, cf. [1].
For the implicit time integration, the determination of the displacement and ve-
locity at time ti+1 includes the acceleration of the considered time step. Thus, the
displacement and velocity are approximated as functions based on the state of the
system in both known and unknown steps. Since the acceleration of the unknown
step is used to compute the state of the unknown step, additional operations must
be introduced in the computation when comparing to the scheme for explicit time
integration. This is especially apparent for non-linear systems. Consequently, the
schemes for implicit time integration are more comprehensive to implement in a
numerical scheme, cf. [1].
It is important to notice that these time integration schemes are approximate solu-
tions where errors will occur and accumulate through the considered time interval.
The algorithms might cause stability issues where the solution diverges with rapid
growth in amplitude. In order to minimize this erroneously increase potential sta-
bility issues, the time discretization must be controlled.
The implicit time integration is less prone to cause amplitude errors and stability
issues than explicit time integration schemes. Thus, the implicit scheme allows
greater time steps as smaller errors are accumulated over the time interval, cf. [1].
Consequently, the use of implicit schemes results in less computational time.
It should be noted, that due to the errors that are often associated with time
integration, these methods should be applied with caution.
In the present report, the implicit time integration method will be used as the
applied scheme. The calculations will be compared to an explicit scheme with a
ﬁne time discretization, in order to assess the implementation of the theory, that
is described in the following section.
58
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
4.3.2
Non-linear Newmark Algorithm
In the present section the implicit non-linear Newmark algorithm is presented. The
algorithm is utilized when computing the response of a multi-degree-of-freedom
system.
The governing EOM for the discretized non-linear multi-dof system with viscous
damping can be written as:
M ¨Vi+1 + C ˙Vi+1 + Q(Vi+1) = Ri+1
(4.34)
For linear systems, the solution procedure utilized in the Newmark algorithm is
established as; 1) The increments of the state vector are computed based on an ap-
proximate integration of the acceleration over the time step and 2) the acceleration
is computed utilizing the EOM.
The state vector at time ti+1 can be expressed as:
Vi+1 = Vi +

(4.35)

Here the Newmark parameters (or tuning parameters) γ and β enables a weighting
of the integration, and thereby a way of controlling the stability and accuracy of
the algorithm, cf. [2].
The state vector of the linear system can be calculated in a single step without
iterations due to the linearity in the system stiﬀness.
This is not the case for
the non-linear system. The non-linear stiﬀness formulation induces errors in the
solution, as described in section 2.8, and a similar iterative procedure needs to be
introduced to solve the non-linear EOMs.
The Non-linear Newmark algorithm is divided into two steps: A predictive step
and a corrective step. The predictive step calculates the state (Vi+1, ˙Vi+1, ¨Vi+1)
at ti+1 as based on the explicit linear acceleration scheme. This is imitated by
applying γ = 0 and β = 0 in the linear Newmark algorithm, cf. [1].
¨Vi+1 = ¨Vi

(4.39)

Due to the non-linearity of the system, the state of equilibrium will not be repre-
sented by the ﬁrst approximation i.e. the applied external load and the internal
forces will not be in equal. Thus, a corrective step needs to be formulated. The
force diﬀerence is quantiﬁed by the residual vector rres:
rres = Ri+1 −M ¨Vi+1 −C ˙Vi+1 −Q(Vi+1)
(4.40)

suﬃciently close to zero.
rres + δrres + . . . = 0
(4.41)

is neglected, cf. [1]. Thus, the linearized residual increment can be formulated in
terms of change in the displacement, velocity and acceleration vectors:

4.3.3
Testing of Algorithm
In the following section the non-linear Newmark algorithm, implemented in BygFEM
is tested. The tests are performed to ensure, that the algorithm provides accurate
results.
Analytic solutions to non-linear dynamic problems rarely apply. Therefore, the
solution obtained by using the Newmark algorithm is compared to the solution
obtained by the CDM algorithm, which has been evaluated in [9]. Furthermore, it
is investigated whether the implementation of the prestressing force yields feasible
results when dynamic analyses are conducted.
Non-linear Newmark Algorithm
The Newmark algorithm is tested for an analysis conducted on the taut simple
cable depicted in Fig. 4.2 before more comprehensive analyses are conducted on
the bridge. The cable is modeled with the Lagrangian beam elements using 21
nodes, resulting in 63 dofs. The cable has an inherent non-linear response and
represents a relevant case study for the kinematic non-linearities. Thus, the cable
is well suited for the testing of the algorithm.

Considering Fig. 4.3 a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between the linear and the non-linear
solution is observed.
For the linear solution, the amplitude continues to increase of the time interval con-
sidered, whereas the non-linear solution has a more bound behavior, cf. [23]. This
behavior can be explained by the coupled deﬂections, where the cable elongates
axially while deﬂecting laterally, as described by the Lagrange strain deﬁnition,
section 2.2. Consequently, the stiﬀness of the cable increases for increasing deﬂec-
tion, and the natural frequency of the cable changes, preventing the amplitude to
increase as the case is for the linear analysis. The non-linear behavior is discussed
further in section 4.6. This supports the statement, that the behavior of the cable
is kinematic non-linear.
In order to validate if the non-linear Newmark algorithm is implemented correctly
in BygFEM, the response of the cable Fig. 4.2 determined using the non-linear
Newmark algorithm is compared to the response of the cable determined using the
explicit non-linear CDM algorithm. The CDM algorithm was initially implemented
in BygFEM from the work performed in [9].
A suﬃciently small time step is used for the two algorithms to remain stable. The
stability and accuracy of the algorithms are discussed further in section 4.3.5.

Prestressing Force
As discussed in section 2.5 the prestressing force contributes to both the element
stiﬀness and the external load.
Therefore, it is highly relevant to check if the
implementation of the prestressing yields a correct response in the element for
dynamic analysis, or if the prestress contribution to the external load yields an
unintentional dynamic response.
In order to investigate if the prestressing force is introduced correctly in a dynamic
analysis, the situations described in Fig. 2.8 case (B) and (C) are considered. Thus,
two dynamic analyses are conducted; (B) where a cantilevered beam is subjected
to prestress Pi alone, and (C) where the cantilevered beam is subjected to axial
load and prestress of equal magnitude Pi = F.
For the two analysis a suﬃciently small time step ∆t is used in order to provide
with stability in the algorithm. The horizontal displacement ux(t) over time t for
the node subjected to the axial load is plotted for both case (B) and (C) in Fig.

at that displacement. The delay that can be observed before the equilibrium is
reached can be explained by the inertia of the structure.

4.3.4
Stability and Accuracy of Algorithm
The stability and accuracy of the Newmark time integration are dependent on the
size of the time step ∆t and the Newmark parameters β and γ.
For the linear Newmark algorithm, speciﬁc relations are derived for the stability
and accuracy, cf. [20]. However, these relations do not apply for the non-linear
Newmark algorithm. Nevertheless, the linear criteria can be used as upper bound
criteria for the non-linear algorithm.
Linear Stability Criteria and Numerical Accuracy
According to [20], the stability of the linear algorithm can be obtained through
diﬀerent approaches, which result in diﬀerent orders of stability; Unconditional
stability, where the stability of the algorithm rests solely on the tuning parameters,
67
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
γ ≥1
2
,
β ≥1
4

γ + 1
2
2
(4.53)
and conditional stability, where the stability of the algorithm is also dependent on
the vibration frequency and the size of the time step:
γ ≥1
2
,
ωn ∆t ≤
1
q
1
4
 γ + 1
2
2 −β
(4.54)
It should be noted that the stability conditions (4.54) must be ensured for all natu-
ral frequencies the discretized model of a given structure. As the discretized model
for e.g. the suspension bridge presented in Chapter 3, contains a great number of
free dofs, vibrations of rather high frequency can in theory occur. Consequently,
rather small time steps must be used in order to ensure the stability of a condition-
ally stable algorithm. Thus, it is convenient to choose γ and β so unconditional
stability is obtained.
Numerical time integration is an approximate solution.
Therefore, a numerical
error will be inherent in the computations. It is relevant to evaluate this error in
order to determine the numerical accuracy of the algorithm. The evaluation of the
accuracy will be in terms of amplitude error and periodic error.
As derived in [20], the amplitude error can be deﬁned by a parameter |λ|. For an
amplitude of 1 in period n, the subsequent period will have an amplitude error of
|λ|. Thereby the amplitude error will accumulate as time elapses. The amplitude
error is deﬁned as:
|λ| =

convenient property and will be used in further work.
In order to address the periodic error, the following approximate formulation from
[20] is utilized.

When the tuning parameters for the Newmark algorithm are chosen to ensure
average acceleration, i.e. γ = 1/2 and β = 1/4, the algorithm is unconditionally
stable and has a periodic error of ∆T/T = 1/12 (ωn∆t).
In the further evaluation of the numerical accuracy, the performance of the New-
mark algorithm is compared to the performance of the CDM, which can be imitated

by the Newmark algorithm, by applying γ = 1/2 and β = 0. The linear CDM al-

periodic error of ∆T/T = 1/24 (ωn∆t)2. It is noticed that this algorithm performs
better in terms of the periodic error, but for high frequent analyses, rather ﬁne
time steps should be used, for the algorithm to remain stable.
4.3.5
Evaluation of Stability and Numerical Accuracy
In the following section stability and numerical accuracy of the non-linear Newmark
algorithm, Algorithm 3, is evaluated.
As discussed in section 4.3.4, the criteria for accuracy and stability for the lin-
ear Newmark algorithm does not apply for the non-linear Newmark algorithm.
Therefore, the cable presented in Fig. 4.2, is considered, in order to investigate
the performance of the non-linear Newmark algorithm in regards to stability and
numerical accuracy.
To assess the inﬂuence from the frequency of the vibration on the numerical accu-
racy of the algorithm, three cases are considered, in which the cable is subjected
to a cyclic unit load that excites the ﬁrst three linear mode shapes. Thus, the load
frequencies are chosen as the linear natural angular frequencies determined from
(4.52), yielding (ω1, ω2, ω3) = (1.60, 6.40, 14.3). The load is imposed on the cable
at mid span, in the 1/4-point, and at mid span respectively, as presented in Fig.
4.8. These will be denoted the ﬁrst, second and third resonant cases.
