Stability
In the present section, the stability of the non-linear CDM and non-linear Newmark
algorithm is evaluated and compared.
According to the linear theory, the Newmark algorithm is unconditionally stable,
whereas the CDM has a stability criterion deﬁned by (4.54). As aforementioned,
the stability criteria must be fulﬁlled for all natural frequencies of the discretized
cable in order to ensure the stability. As the cable includes 59 free dofs in total, the

while still securing stability.

Numerical Accuracy
In the present section, the numerical accuracy of the non-linear Newmark algorithm
is evaluated.
In order to evaluate the numerical accuracy of the Newmark algorithm, diﬀerent
time steps ∆t are used to determine the response for the situations presented in

the attacking point of the load and compared to a solution in the converged state
with respect to both number of elements, nel, iteration residual εr and time step
magnitude ∆t = 0.001. The analyses are conducted over a time span of 50 s, but
in the present section, the response is considered for approximately 12 periods as
presented in Fig. 4.10 to 4.12. The response for the full 50 s are presented section
4.6.
The response of the ﬁrst resonant case, Fig. 4.8(a), is presented in Fig. 4.10.

Fig. 4.10: Time step dependency for the ﬁrst resonant case.
From Fig. 4.10 it is observed, that the solution converges for decreasing magnitude
of the time step, and the response for ∆t = T1/40 = 0.1 s (
) has reached a

As opposed to the criteria for the stability, the accuracy should only be fulﬁlled
for natural frequencies that contribute signiﬁcantly to the vibration, cf. [21]. In
order to assess the signiﬁcance of each mode shape associated with the natural
frequencies, the mechanical energy of the vibrating system can be considered.
As a rule of thumb, the accuracy of the response is satisfying if the modes in which
90% of the mechanical energy of the vibrating system is stored. Thus, the time step
∆t should be chosen based on the period TJ where J describes the highest mode
for which 90% of the total energy is stored, cf. [21]. Naturally, a numerical error
will be introduced for higher frequency modes. However, as these modes do not
contribute signiﬁcantly to the system vibration, V(t), the error will not inﬂuence
the response.
In this simple example, the cable is excited in compliance to mode one. Therefore,
71
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
the majority of the total energy of the vibrating system will be stored in mode one,
and J = 1. Thus, the time step can be evaluated based on the period for mode one
T1. Had the vibration included more complex vibration modes an analysis should
be conducted in order to evaluate which modes are signiﬁcant.
Considering the non-converged solutions in Fig. 4.10, where the time steps ∆t =
T1/15 (
) and ∆t = T1/7 (
) are used, it is noted that the overall behavior of
the response diverges from the solution. First of all, an increasing periodic error
is observed when the time step size is increased. Furthermore, it is noted that the
oscillation of the cable builds up and dies out at a lower rate meaning, that the
number of oscillations observed within a single beat (see section 4.6) is increased
with the decreasing time step magnitude. Finally, it is observed that the amplitude
of the response is both over- and underestimated at given points in time for the
non-converged solutions.
The great change in response observed when using the time steps ∆t = T1/15 and
∆t = T1/7 could be explained by the discretization of the stiﬀness, as the overall
stiﬀness of the structure could then be underestimated resulting in the decreased
frequency of the response and the overestimation of the amplitude. As the non-
converged solutions to the response are not considered in the continued analysis,
further analysis has not been conducted regarding this behavior.
In order to investigate the frequency dependency of the numerical accuracy of the
non-linear Newmark algorithm, the response of the second and third resonant cases
are considered. The responses for the two cases are presented in Fig. 4.11 and Fig.
4.12 respectively.

From Fig. 4.11 and Fig. 4.12 it is observed, that the solution converges for de-

It is worth noting, that the time step required to reach convergence is highly
dependent on the frequency of the vibration considered. As the frequency of the
vibration is increased from ω1 to ω3, the time step must be reduced in size to
prevent the increase of the periodic error. This is in compliance with the linear
expression for the periodic error (4.56).
Considering the response determined using the time steps TJ/7 (
), where J is the
mode that is imitated, a rather large reduction of the amplitude is observed. This
amplitude error is not assessed as being caused by inaccuracy in the algorithm, but
rather by poor discretization of the curve.
In conclusion, a time step of TJ/40 seems to be satisfying for the three cases
considered in this section. It is, however, important to stress, that these analyses
have been conducted for rather simple cases, where the cable has been loaded to
resonate in compliance with mode shapes one too three, giving a good idea of in
which modes the energy is stored.
For an analysis of e.g. a full bridge, the response can be rather complex, and a
great number of modes can be signiﬁcant to the vibration. Therefore, the time
step magnitude can be diﬃcult to evaluate beforehand, and a convergence analysis
should always be conducted. Here ∆t = TJ/40 can be used as a reasonable initial
guess for the time step magnitude.
Finally, it should be noted, that it is not always relevant to minimize the periodic
error, and greater time steps might yield satisfying results for some cases.

4.4
Non-linear Modal Analysis Using Reduced Basis by
Modal Derivatives
For discretized structures that include a great number of dofs, the use of time
integration methods can prove inconvenient due to the small time step needed to
reach reasonable accuracy. Therefore, the advantage of selected basis reduction
methods, presented in [9], are investigated.
The basis reduction methods can prove beneﬁcial, as the EOMs are projected from
the three-dimensional coordinate system (x, y, z) onto a N dimensional subspace
spanned by the basis vectors. Thus, the EOMs can be solved in the subspace,
reducing the number of equations to be solved while retaining the accuracy of the
solution. Furthermore, the basis reduction methods provide a method of removing
high-frequency behavior from the solution, thereby reducing the criteria for stabil-
ity and accuracy providing the possibility of using greater time steps ∆t than in
the time integration algorithms, cf. [9].
The notion of basis reduction methods has brieﬂy been described in section 4.2 in
terms of modal analysis. Here the response V(t) is usually described in terms of
N, where N represents all mode shapes for the discretized structure. However, as
aforementioned, V(t) can be described using a reduced number of mode shapes,
i.e. a subspace represented by a reduced basis spanned by a reduced set of basis
vectors. This feature is highly utilized in the basis reduction methods, therefore N
will no longer denote the number of available mode shapes, but the number of basis
vectors introduced in the reduced basis. Thus the response for the linear modal
analysis can be described as:
V(t) ≈
N
X
j=1
ϕjsj(t)
(4.58)
As seen from (4.58) the linear response can be determined from the mode shapes
and modal coordinates alone, by decoupling the EOMs.
According to [9], the
EOMs can be decoupled for the non-linear analysis as well. This would, however,
require, that the basis is updated for each time step, which would increase the
computational time signiﬁcantly. Furthermore, it is relevant to consider, that the
axial strain is highly relevant to lateral deﬂections in non-linear theory, as described
by the Lagrange strain measure (2.5). This means, that for non-linear theory, the
transverse modes are coupled to axial modes, and according to [9] omitting these
coupled modes from the analysis can lead to locking, which can potentially ruin
the accuracy of the response.
Thus, a basis reduction method suitable for the
non-linear analysis must be used.
In the following sections, the theory surrounding basis reduction methods using lin-
ear modes shapes and modal derivatives are explained. Furthermore, the implemen-
tation of the basis reduction methods in the locally based FE-program BygFEM
74
==PAGE:97==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
and the stability and accuracy of the algorithm is checked, in order to ensure that
accurate results are obtained using these methods.
4.4.1
Taylor Expansion of Displacement Field and Modal Deriva-
tives
In order to develop an eﬃcient reduced basis for the kinematic non-linear structure,
it is relevant to include the kinematic non-linearity of the elements. The applied
non-linear beam element has a coupling between the lateral and axial strains, as
described by the Lagrangian strain measure (2.5). It is therefore relevant, that
the modes used in the basis reduction can represent the coupling activated defor-
mations, cf. [23]. One method for evaluating the deformations activated due to
the coupling eﬀects is by including modal derivatives, which represent higher order
eﬀects, in the system.
According to [23] a higher order displacement ﬁeld can be chosen as a solution to
the kinematic non-linear EOMs. In the present case the displacement ﬁeld V(t),
presented in (4.58), is expanded as a Taylor series in the reduced coordinates sj,
and the terms up to the second order are included:

The reduced subspace of the displacement ﬁeld V, can be written in the classical

(4.72)

==PAGE:100==

Here Φ contains the linear mode shapes and their associated derivatives, while s
contains the modal coordinate si and the second-order product of modal coordi-
nates sisj. Thus, as the linear mode shapes and the higher-order modal derivatives
are not independent of each other, and the basis vectors spanning the reduced sub-
space are not orthogonal, the response can no longer be described as uncoupled.
In order to describe the reduced subspace (4.72), two diﬀerent approaches are con-
sidered; 1) A reduced basis where the linear mode shapes and the corresponding
modal derivative terms are assumed being orthogonal with independent modal co-
ordinates in s, and 2) by performing an additional transformation of the already
reduced basis, where the modal derivatives are projected onto an additionally re-
duced subspace in which the modal derivatives are orthogonal, thus decoupling the
response fully.
4.4.2
Reduced Basis Assuming Independent Modal Coordinates
In the following section, it is assumed that the basis vectors that span the reduced
basis in (4.72) are uncoupled. This is not the case, and a part of the modal deriva-
tives can be obtained from linear combinations of other modal derivatives. Thus,
these vectors must be removed from the basis in order to ensure orthogonality.
The method for removing unwanted modal derivatives from the reduced basis is
explained later in the section. The method is referred to as RBIC.
Assuming that all modal derivatives are uncoupled, the reduced basis can be ex-
pressed as in 4.73. Here the ﬁrst N terms in s denotes the modal coordinates for the
linear mode shapes, and the next H terms denotes the modal for the coordinates
modal derivatives.
Φ = [ ϕ1 ϕ2 ... ϕN ∂ϕ11 ∂ϕ21 ... ∂ϕNN ]
s = [ s1 s2 ... sN+H ]
(4.73)
As aforementioned the modal derivatives, that can be described as a linear combi-
nation of existing modal derivatives must be removed from the reduced basis. This
is in order to avoid singularities in the solution when solving for the unknowns, cf.
[23]. An example of linear dependent basis vectors, are the ones containing the the
same modal derivatives, e.g. the basis vectors ∂ϕ12 and ∂ϕ21, that both contain
the derivatives of ϕ1 and ϕ2. This group of linearly dependent modal derivatives
is excluded from the reduced basis simply, by introducing i instead of N in the
second summation of the modal derivatives as done in 4.71.
To prevent that unwanted basis vectors are included in the reduced basis, each
of the modal derivatives ∂ϕij are evaluated. Initially, all of the basis vectors are
removed from the basis. The basis matrix from which the modal derivative has
been removed is now referred to as Φij. The basis vectors are then considered one
at a time. If the considered modal derivative can be described approximately as
a linear combination of the basis vectors contained in Φij, the modal derivative is
78
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
not included in the ﬁnal basis matrix. Otherwise, the basis vector is incorporated
in Φij. This is checked as:
∂ϕij ≈Φij βij
(4.74)
Here βij contains the coeﬃcient in the linear relation. Due to the numerical in-
accuracies in the determination of the linear regression in (4.74), an evaluation of
the linear dependency is developed. A parameter e determines the accuracy of
the approximation in (4.74) by evaluating the length of the diﬀerence between the
considered modal derivative and the reduced basis divided by the length of the
modal derivative.
e =
∂ϕij −Φij βij

∥∂ϕij∥
(4.75)
If e exceeds 1% no linear dependency between the considered modal derivative and
the basis vectors in Φij exist, and the basis vector is incorporated in the reduced
basis. Contrarily, if e < 1% the considered modal derivatives can be described by a
linear combination of the basis vectors in Φij, and the considered modal derivative
is removed from the reduced basis.
A pseudo algorithm for the reduced basis method is presented schematically in
Algorithm 4. The implementation of the RBIC-method in BygFEM is presented
in Appendix R.5.
79
==PAGE:102==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
Algorithm 4 RBIC applying the non-linear Newmark Algorithm
1: % Deﬁne number of considered mode shapes N, Newmark parameters γ, β,
time step size ∆t, number of time steps M and error tolerance εr.
2: % Build the mass matrices M, damping matrix C and force vector R.
3: % Determine mode shapes ϕi, modal derivatives ∂ϕij, and organize the basis

4.4.3
Taylor Basis
In the following section, the assumption that the modal derivatives are uncoupled
is not made.
Consequently, a part of the modal derivatives contained in Φ is
coupled. This characteristic is dealt with by adding an additional transformation of
the modal derivatives, thus projecting them onto a reduced subspace in which they
are orthogonal, thereby decoupling the response fully, and reducing the number of
equations to be solved to N. This approach will be denoted the Taylor Basis or
RBTB.
The reduced basis, (4.71), can be formulated, as compact basis formulation, similar
to (4.72):
V(s) = ΦT sT
(4.76)
The linear mode shapes and the associated modal derivatives are organized in the
Taylor basis matrix ΦT , and the N modal coordinates and the H second order
coordinate products are organized in the Taylor coordinate vector sT , cf. [24].
ΦT = [ ϕ1 ϕ2 ... ϕN ∂ϕ11 ∂ϕ21 ... ∂ϕNN ]
sT = [ s1 s2 ... sN s1s1 s2s1 s2s2 ... sNsN ]
(4.77)
Projecting the EOMs for the dynamic system onto the Taylor Basis yields the
following formulation;
ˆm¨sT + ˆc ˙sT + ˆq(sT ) = ˆf (t)
(4.78)
Here the mass matrix ˆm, damping matrix ˆc, nodal force vector ˆq and load vector
ˆf are deﬁned as:
ˆm = (ΦT )T M ΦT
ˆc = (ΦT )T C ΦT
ˆq(sT ) = (ΦT )T q ΦT sT
ˆf (t) = (ΦT )T F(t)
(4.79)
According to [24], the system equations in (4.78) include second order terms in the
unknown coordinates (si, ˙si, ¨si). In order to solve the system, the equations must
only include terms of the ﬁrst order, and the system is transformed from Taylor
basis (sT , ˙sT ,¨sT ) to the (s, ˙s, ¨s) coordinates. This can be done by introducing the
transformation matrices Qs(s), U(s) and P(˙s), cf. [24].
sT = Qs(s)s
(4.80)
˙sT = U(s)˙s
(4.81)
¨sT = U(s)¨s + 2 P (˙s)˙s)
(4.82)
81
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
Thus, the EOMs are transformed into the (s, ˙s, ¨s) coordinates:
¯m(s)¨s, + ¯c(s, ˙s) ˙s + ¯q(s) = ¯f(s, t)
(4.83)
where the mass matrix ¯m(s), damping matrix ¯c(s, ˙s), nodal force vector ¯q(s) and
load vector ¯f(s, t) are deﬁned as:
¯m(s) = U(s)T ˆmU(s)
¯c(s, ˙s) = U(s)T (2 ˆmP(˙s) + ˆcU(s)
¯q(s) = U(s)T ˆq(sT )
¯f(s, t) = U(s)Tˆf(t)
(4.84)
In order to describe the relation between the two coordinate bases, (sT , ˙sT , ¨sT ),
the (s, ˙s, ¨s), the transformation matrices will brieﬂy be introduced. It is worth
noting, that these are dependent on the variables (s, ˙s), and should be evaluated
between each time step, cf. Appendix A in [24].
The transformation matrix U(s) is deﬁned as:
U(s) = H + R(s)
,
R(s) =

An important characteristic of the Taylor basis is, that the second order modal
coordinates are obtained as products of the linear modal coordinates. Therefore,
no additional unknowns are introduced when the system equations are solved for
82
==PAGE:105==

the modal derivatives. Thus, the linear stability criterion appears to apply for the
numerical solver used to obtain the modal coordinates, cf. [9].
A pseudo algorithm for the RBTB is presented schematically in Algorithm 5. The
implementation of the RBTB-method in BygFEM is presented in Appendix R.6.
The RBTB-method can further optimized as presented in [9]. Here the transforma-
tion matrices are redeﬁned with a more suitable summation. Furthermore, a more
eﬃcient formulation of the internal restoring forces is presented in [9]. The main
concept is to manipulate the global internal restoring force into a vector polynomial
to reduce the assembling of the vector in each iteration step.

4.4.4
Testing of Algorithm
In the following section, the reduced basis methods implemented in BygFEM are
tested. The tests are performed to ensure, that the algorithms provide accurate
results. The solution obtained by using the reduced basis algorithms is compared
to the solution obtained by the Newmark algorithm, which has been evaluated in
section 4.3.3. Furthermore, it is investigated whether the implementation of the
prestressing force yields feasible results when dynamic analyses are conducted.
Simple Cable Case
The analyses are conducted considering the taut cable, that is depicted in Fig.
4.2. The check regarding the implementation of the basis reduction methods is
conducted by comparing the response obtained from the reduced basis method to
the response obtained using the non-linear Newmark algorithm. Furthermore, the
modal derivatives are determined and compared to the modal derivatives obtained
in [9]. This is followed by an evaluation of the derived modal coordinates, where
it is checked if these fulﬁll the second-order Taylor series expansion.
The response computed using RBIC and the response computed using the RBTB,
are compared to the response computed using the non-linear Newmark algorithm
and presented in Fig. 4.13. A suﬃcient number of linear mode shapes N and their
associated derived mode shapes are used. The vertical deformation uy,m(t) at the
center point of the taut cable is determined as a function of time t and normalized
with the maximum displacement umax.

Implementation of Prestress
The theory for the basis reduction is derived without the implementation of pre-
stress. Therefore, it is highly relevant to check if the implementation of the pre-
stressing yields a correct response in the element for a dynamic analysis when the
basis reduction methods are used. The tests are conducted by evaluating the ver-
tical response of the cantilevered beam presented in Fig. 4.6, and comparing it to
the response determined using the Newmark algorithm.
The vertical response computed using the RBTB at the beam tip, uy(t), is pre-
sented in Fig.
4.18 and compared to the response computed by the Newmark
algorithm.

4.4.5
Evaluation of Stability and Numerical Accuracy for Re-
duced Basis Methods
In the following section stability and numerical accuracy of the reduced basis meth-
ods, Algorithm 4 and Algorithm 5, are evaluated.
The stability of the RBIC is governed by the numerical solver used for determining
the modal coordinates, i.e. the non-linear Newmark algorithm. As no problems
were observed for the stability of the non-linear Newmark algorithm, within time
step sizes yielding meaningful results, the stability is not considered further for this
method.
The stability of the RBTB is, according to [9], governed by the linear stability
criterion for the numerical solver used for determining the modal coordinates. As
the non-linear Newmark algorithm is used as numerical solver, the RBTB should
be unconditionally stable.
Thus, only the numerical accuracy of the basis reductions methods are considered.
This is done by considering the eﬀect of the time step size ∆t and number of mode
shapes N and corresponding modal derivatives.
Reduced Basis Assuming Independent Modal Coordinates
In the present section the numerical accuracy of the RBIC-method is considered.
The accuracy is evaluated by considering the response of the taut cable presented
in Fig. 4.2.
90
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
Initially, the response for the cable is determined using three diﬀerent time step
sizes. It is ensured that solutions are converged with respect to the number of
linear modes and associated modal derivatives included in the reduced basis. The
solutions to the responses are depicted in Fig. 4.19 along with a converged solution
for the response determined using the non-linear Newmark algorithm.

As aforementioned, the time integration method implemented in the reduced ba-
sis methods is the non-linear Newmark algorithm. Therefore, the time step size
required for ensuring a satisfying numerical accuracy should be identical to the
time step size ensuring numerical accuracy for the non-linear Newmark algorithm,
which is presented in section 4.3.5.
Here it was observed, that a time step of TJ/40 should be used to ensure the
numerical accuracy. As the excitation frequency is a linear combination of the two
ﬁrst linear mode shapes, it is deduced, that the vibration is governed by the ﬁrst
two mode shapes, and the time step magnitude should be at least T2/40 = 0.02 s.
This is in compliance with the performed analysis, as depicted in Fig. 4.19, where
the solution is approximately converged at ∆t = 0.02 s (
).
The response of the taut cable is now considered for a time step size resulting in
a converged solution. The response is determined while including diﬀerent quan-
tities of mode shapes in the basis, in order to assess the required number of mode
shapes, that should be included to reach a satisfying numerical accuracy for the so-
lution. The diﬀerent solutions to the response are depicted in Fig. 4.20, along with
a converged solution for the response determined using the non-linear Newmark
algorithm.

From Fig. 4.20 it is observed that, including one linear mode shape and the associ-
ated modal derivatives in the reduced basis, results in an inaccurate determination
of the response, when comparing to the converged response (
). This is because
the reduced basis does not contain the necessary mode shapes and modal deriva-
tives to represent the non-linear behavior of the cable induced by the present load
case.
Furthermore, it is observed from Fig 4.20, that including N = 2 linear mode shapes
and associated modal derivatives in the reduced basis, (
), yields a reasonable
solution for the response. This can be explained by considering the exciting load
from (4.51).
As this is a linear combination of the ﬁrst two mode shapes, the
response is governed by these. Thus, the basis contains the governing mode shapes
and associated modal derivatives.
As the solution is not converged entirely by including the ﬁrst two linear mode
shapes in the reduced basis, N = 3 is considered, (
) in Fig 4.20. It is observed,
that the solution to the response converges further and is approximately identical
to the converged solution determined from the non-linear Newmark algorithm.
Consequently, the number of linear mode shapes and associated derivatives in-
cluded in the reduced basis must be analyzed for each considered case to ensure
the accuracy in the results. However, considering the character of the exciting
load, can give an indication of which mode shapes should be included in the basis
to reach reasonable solutions for simple structures.

4.5
Evaluation of Computational Methods Applied in
Non-linear Structural Dynamics
In the following section, the computational time of Algorithm 3, Algorithm 4 and
Algorithm 5 used for the non-linear dynamic analysis will be evaluated. The com-
putational eﬃciency of non-linear Newmark algorithm and the reduced basis al-
gorithms are compared to the computational eﬃciency of the non-linear CDM-
method. Subsequently, the eﬃciency of the Newmark algorithm is compared to
the reduced basis methods to investigate if the possibility of only considering the
signiﬁcant modes of the vibration.
Finally, the computational eﬃciency of the
reduced basis methods is discussed in regards to stability.
For the following analysis the computational eﬃciency is measured as CPU-time
used for determining the response for the taut cable presented in Fig. 4.2, which
is discretized using 21 nodes. The response determined using the four diﬀerent
methods are presented in Fig. 4.23.

From the results presented in table 4.2 it is apparent, that the methods in which
the non-linear Newmark algorithm is used for the time integration are signiﬁcantly
more eﬃcient than the non-linear CDM algorithm.
This can be explained by
considering the time step required for ensuring the stability of the CDM algorithm,
which is signiﬁcantly smaller than the time steps required for ensuring reasonable
accuracy in the non-linear Newmark algorithm, as discussed in section 4.3.4.
Considering table 4.2, it is noted that the reduced basis methods reduce the compu-
tational time further when comparing to the non-linear Newmark algorithm. This
can be explained by the reduction of the number of governing equations that must
be solved for the reduced basis methods compared to for the non-linear Newmark
algorithm, as these methods enable disregarding high-frequency behavior of less
signiﬁcance from the analysis.
95
==PAGE:118==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
Finally, considering table 4.2, it is noted that the RBIC-method is more eﬃcient
than the RBTB-method in terms of CPU-time. As presented in section 4.4 the
number of governing equations that must be solved for the RBIC-method is equal
to the number of N linear modes and their H associated modal derivatives, while
it for the RBTB-method is N meaning, that the number of equations that must be
solved is reduced. The additional basis reduction performed in the RBTB-method
does, however, require comprehensive matrix operations, which explains why the
CPU-time is not reduced further. Thus, the incentive of introducing the additional
basis in the Taylor method is only apparent when considering the stability of the
time integration method used in the reduced basis methods. For the RBIC the
stability rests on the N number of linear mode shapes and the associated number
of modal derivatives while the stability of the RBTB solely rests on the N number of
linear mode shapes. Therefore, the requirement for stability in the time integration
scheme implemented in the basis reduction methods is reduced when using the
RBTB, and greater time steps could be used. However, as presented in table 4.2, a
time step size of ∆t = 0.2 s is used for both the non-linear Newmark algorithm and
the reduced basis methods. This can be explained by the fact that instability of
the Newmark algorithm has not been observed for time steps yielding meaningful
solutions to the response. Thus, the beneﬁt of introducing the additional basis
reduction in the RBTB remains absent when comparing to RBIC.
Situations exist where implementing implicit time integration methods, such as
the non-linear Newmark algorithm, in the reduced basis methods is not feasible.
Such a situation is presented in [9], where real-time simulations in hybrid testing
are considered. Thus, an explicit algorithm is normally used for real-time hybrid
testing, why the RBTB-method is highly eﬃcient for these types of tests, as the
stability of the time integration method is critical.
Until now only the taut cable discretized by 21 nodes and 59 free dofs has been
considered.
As aforementioned one of the beneﬁts associated with the reduced
basis methods is, that high-frequency behavior can be disregarded. For greater
and more complex structures a lot of high frequencies of low signiﬁcance may be
included in the time integration methods. Thus, the increase in computational
eﬃciency associated with using RBIC and RBTB instead of the non-linear Newark
algorithm should be more pronounced for structures including a greater number of
dofs. Therefore the eﬀect of increasing the discretization of the taut cable is investi-
gated. The CPU-time for determining the response using the non-linear Newmark
algorithm, the RBIC-method and the RBTB-method is depicted as functions of
the number of dofs in the taut cable in Fig. 4.24, along with the reduction of
CPU-time achieved by using the reduced basis methods.

Considering Fig. 4.24(a) it is noted that the increase in CPU-time of the response

supported by the result presented in Fig. 4.24(b), where it is apparent, that the
diﬀerence in CPU-time between the non-linear Newmark algorithm and the reduced
basis methods increases quite rapidly when a great number of dofs is used in the
taut cable. Further and more thorough analyses should be conducted to verify
this behavior, but it is concluded that the basis reduction methods are likely to
perform superior to the non-linear Newmark algorithm for structures including a
great number of dofs.
4.6
Considerations Regarding Non-linear Behavior
The purpose of the present section is to evaluate parts of the behavior asso-
ciated with the non-linear resonance of a simple structure and familiarize with
these. Therefore the taut cable analysis conducted in section 4.3.5 for the three
resonant load cases in phase with the linear natural frequencies (ω1, ω2, ω3) =
(1.60, 6.40, 14.3) is revisited for an increased period of time considered. It should
be noted, that the frequency of the excitation is kept constant at the linear fre-
quency throughout the analysis.
The vertical deﬂection uy(t) at the point of loading is presented in Fig. 4.25 for
the ﬁrst resonant case, for a time interval of 50 s. The analysis is performed for a
converged state regarding number of elements, nel, iteration tolerance εr and time
step magnitude ∆t.

A notable diﬀerence is observed between the linear and non-linear analysis. For
the linear case with load excitation frequency equal to the linear natural frequency
ωe = ω1, the cable and load will resonate and the amplitude will have an unbound
linear growth, cf. [25]. However, this is not the case for the non-linear analysis.
The equations governing the frequencies of the non-linear response is found by
introducing the tangent stiﬀness KT (V) in the generalized eigenvalue problem:
 KT (V) −ω2 M

ϕ = 0
(4.92)
From (4.92) it is noted that that the natural frequency of the non-linear structure
is dependent on the displacement V. Considering the non-linear response in Fig.
4.25 (
), this is expressed as the non-linear response deviating from the linear
response. It is noted that the non-linear response initially follows the response
of the linear analysis reasonable. As the displacement V increases, so does the
natural frequency of the cable, i.e. ω1,non.lin ≥ωe. This results in the phenomenon
known as beats, cf. [25]. Beats occur for the linear response when the excitation
frequency is close to the natural frequency of the system. The concept is presented
for ωe = 1.15 ω1 in Fig. 4.25, (
).
According to [1] closed-form solutions to non-linear problems rarely exist. Thus, the
response of non-linear structures may be evaluated by the linear beat phenomenon.
The beat response is composed of a second order product of sine functions, where
a rapid oscillation of frequency | ωe + ω1 |/2 is enclosed by a slower oscillation of
frequency | ωe −ω1 |/2.
A close resemblance between the non-linear response for ωe = ω1 and the linear
response for ωe = 1.15 ω1 is observed.
Similar beat phenomena occur for the non-linear analysis of the second and third
resonant case, as presented in Fig. 4.26 and 4.27.

An additional oscillation seems to occur for the taut cable excited in compliance
with mode two and three. For the response, it is noted, that an additional sine
function encloses the primary beat phenomenon. Due to the higher frequency of the
second and third mode shape, more periods will obviously occur, while the response
amplitude will decrease, due to the inverse proportionality between amplitude and
frequency, cf. [1].
For linear systems, the properties associated with resonance are well described.
This is not the case for non-linear systems, as resonant behavior of non-linear
systems is much more comprehensive, cf. [26].
In linear dynamics, the individual uncoupled mode resonates at a unique frequency.
In non-linear dynamics, however, it is possible to excite sub-harmonic resonances
when the excitation frequency approaches a multiple or fraction of the natural
frequencies, cf. [27].
Another phenomenon in non-linear resonance theory is period-doubling bifurcation.
The phenomena occur when a slight change in the dynamic parameters results in
the system shifts to an alternative behavior with twice the natural frequency of
99
==PAGE:122==
Chapter 4.
Theoretical Basis for Linear and Non-linear Dynamic FE-Analysis
the original system. Thereby a small perturbation of the structural system might
course the period-doubling bifurcation of the vibration. This small disturbance can
be caused by e.g. the stiﬀness increase in the vibration modes.
Consequently, it is deduced that structures of non-linear character may result in a
more complex response behavior than structures of linear character.

==PAGE:123==
Chapter 5
Case Study for Evaluation of
Non-linear Dynamics
In the present chapter, a dynamic analysis of IBB will be conducted.
As for
the static case, Chapter 3, the bridge will be modeled as a discrete plane beam
model using BygFEM. The model will be evaluated in terms of structural damping
and dynamic loading. In order to determine the vibration characteristics of the
IBB, a linear frequency analysis is conducted, and compared to experimental test
for veriﬁcation of the numerical model. In order to solve the general non-linear
dynamic problem, diﬀerent solving techniques are applied, as presented in the
theoretical basis, Chapter 4. The reduced basis methods are compared to the non-
linear Newmark method, in terms of validity of the displacement of the considered
structural model. Finally, a non-linear analysis will be conducted for the oscillating
prestressed bridge in order to determine the displacement, frequency development
and stress state. A comparative study is conducted between the linear and non-
linear dynamic analysis to evaluate the eﬀects of non-linearity.
5.1
Basis for Non-linear Dynamic Analysis of IBB
In the following sections, the basis for the non-linear dynamic analysis of the IBB
is established. Initially, the numerical model used in the dynamic non-linear anal-
ysis is explained. Furthermore, the dynamic load model used for the analysis is
explained.
This is followed by a discussion regarding the damping applied for
the dynamic analysis. Finally, a linear frequency analysis is conducted in order
to establish preliminary parameters to be used in the further non-linear dynamic
analysis of the IBB.
5.1.1
Numerical Model
The model used for the dynamic analysis is identical to the plane non-linear beam
model, that was evaluated in the static analysis, section 3.3. The same assumptions
101
==PAGE:124==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
apply, as well as identical topology and meshing are used. The bridge is subjected
to the prestress and permanent loads presented in section 3.5. The permanent
load; structural and non-structural, is collected in the mass matrix M in order to
include the contributions in the inertia term of the EOM, while the driving forces
are collected in R(t).
5.1.2
Load Models for Dynamic Analysis
As the spans of large bridges increase, the dynamic load situations become more
critical in a bridge design situation. Diﬀerent dynamic load scenarios aﬀect the
bridge structure, but in the following section, the dynamic wind forces will be in
focus. The wind load will excite resonant responses, often in several modes of the
structure.
According to [28] several mechanisms at various wind speed can cause a bridge
deck of long span bridges to resonate.
Here under vortex shedding excitation,
ﬂutter instability and buﬀeting excitation are mentioned.
Vortex shedding excitation is a mechanism where cross wind vibrations occur due
to ﬂuctuating load arising behind bluﬀcross-sectional shapes. Vortex shedding
excitation is common at low wind speeds and low turbulence conditions.
Flutter instability mechanisms cover diﬀerent types of aeroelastic instabilities. The
most commonly understood used of the term ﬂutter is a coupled translational-
rotational instability, cf.
[28].
The ﬂutter instability occurs at very high wind
speeds for aerodynamically stable decks.
Buﬀeting excitation is caused by the ﬂuctuating forces induced by natural turbu-
lence and gustiness of the oncoming wind. This phenomenon occurs over a wide
range of wind speeds and normally increases monotonically with increasing wind
speed, cf. [28].
In the following sections, only the phenomenon of vortex shedding will be con-
sidered as load scenario. Vortex shedding is a self-containing phenomenon, which
rarely results in a chaotic behavior [6]. The vortex shedding is a ﬂuid-structural
phenomenon, where the occurrence of a periodic shedding of ﬂuid eddies is formed
from the rolling up of the boundary layer shed from a bluﬀbody, cf. [28] and a vor-
tex street forms behind the body. This results in a harmonic cross wind excitation
with frequencies, that coincide with the vibration modes of the bridge.
This wind-structure interaction seems to lock in through a feedback mechanism.
The frequency of the shedding of vortices synchronizes to the frequency of the
motion of the bridge deck, with a mutual enhanced eﬀect, cf. [28].
The vortex shedding load actions on the bridge structure can be simpliﬁed, by
applying a sinusoidal excitation model, [6]. Here it is assumed, that the shedding of
vortices generates a cyclic crosswind force with a sinusoidal variation. Furthermore,
a full correlation of the forces over the cross-sectional height and constant amplitude
of the ﬂuctuating crosswind force coeﬃcient are assumed. This is not a particularly
102
==PAGE:125==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
accurate model, but it is useful for determining maximum amplitudes for crosswind
vibrations caused by vortex shedding.
Due to the lock-in phenomenon of the vortex shedding it is assumed, that the
phase in which the shedding of vortices occur, and the magnitude of the equivalent
force on the bridge deck "locks" to the modes of the bridge. Consequently, the
sinusoidal loading p(t) on the bridge coincide with the natural frequency of the
bridge. Thereby, the sinusoidal load is applied in compliance with the normalized
mode shape, p(t) ϕi/ϕi,max, as depicted for an arbitrary mode shape:
ϕi
ϕi,max p(t)
Fig. 5.1: Cyclic dynamic loading in compliance with ith mode shape ϕi.
5.1.3
Structural Damping of IBB
Diﬀerent damping models exist, and in the following section, the Rayleigh Damping
will be presented.
The undamped modes, determined from a modal analysis, result in an uncoupling of
the response, as the mass matrix M and stiﬀness matrix K are orthogonal. Thereby
the implementation of an orthogonal damping matrix C will retain the uncoupled
behavior for the response. Rayleigh damping is a simple method for implementation
of orthogonal modal damping since the damping matrix C is a linear combination
of the mass- and stiﬀness matrix, cf. [20]. Due to the orthogonality, the modal
damping c can be established as:
C = α M + β K ,
c = ΦT C Φ = α m + β k
(5.1)
According to [20] the modal damping ratio ζj corresponding to the speciﬁc mode
j, can be established as:
2 ζj =
α
ωj
+ β ωj
,
j = 1 , 2 , ... , N
(5.2)
The modal damping ratio consists of a mass term, which is inversely proportional
to the natural angular frequency ωj, and a stiﬀness term, which is proportional to
ωj. Thus, the modal damping ratio is frequency dependent, and the damping of
the lower frequencies are governed by the mass, whereas the damping of the higher
frequencies is governed by the stiﬀness.
103
==PAGE:126==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
The Rayleigh damping will attain a level of minimum damping ζmin at the fre-
quency ωmin. This global minimum can be used to evaluate the structural damping,
with the following relation, cf. [1]:
ζmin =
p
α β
,
ωmin =
p
α/β
(5.3)
The Rayleigh damping is assumed to be the viscous damping of the bridge struc-
ture. It is assumed, that the bridge has a logarithmic decrement of δ ≃ζ 2 π =
2 %, cf. [29], on the lowest eigenfrequency, as presented in table 5.1. The Rayleigh
parameters, are determined from (5.3), see Appendix N:
α = 0.0023
,
β = 0.0044
(5.4)
It should be recognized, that damping is usually one of the more uncertain parts
in practical dynamic analysis, and extensive investigations should be conducted to
determine precise damping properties for the bridge.
5.1.4
Linear Frequency Analysis
As a preliminary dynamic analysis of the bridge, a linear modal analysis is con-
ducted. The linear modal analysis contributes to knowledge of the eigenfrequencies
of the bridge and the corresponding mode shapes. This gives a reasonable basis
for veriﬁcation of the model when considering the dynamic behavior. Furthermore,
the eigenfrequencies and mode shapes are of high importance in further analysis,
as the load model is based on these properties, as discussed in section 5.1.2.
The mode shapes of the free vibration ϕj, are determined from the generalized
eigenvalue problem, see (4.92). The linear modes shapes are depicted in Fig. 5.2.
The mode shapes are presented in full size in Appendix O. It should once again be
noted that the considered model is a plane beam. Thus, only the vertical modes
of vibration are analyzed. Torsional modes of vibration exist as well. These are
highly relevant to consider e.g. the modeling of ﬂutter instabilities, but will not be
analyzed in the present report.

The bending modes can be divided into symmetrical and antisymmetrical modes of
vibration. Mode two and four are symmetrical and mode one, three, ﬁve and six are
antisymmetrical. As opposed to the vibration modes for a simple beam or a taut
cable, it is noted that the ﬁrst mode shape for the bridge is antisymmetric. This
coincides with the relation presented in Fig. 5.3. Here it is observed, that the order
of symmetric and antisymmetric mode shapes shifts when the sagging k in cables
increases. Consequently, the ﬁrst modes shape of the sagged cable is antisymmetric.
This behavior can be explained by the fact, that either an elongation of the cable
or a deﬂection of the pylons is necessary for the ﬁrst symmetric mode to occur,
whereas the antisymmetric mode shape requires less or zero extension of the cable
and displacement of the pylons, cf. [30].

5.2
Evaluation of Reduced Basis Algorithms for IBB
Analysis
In the following sections the performance of the reduced basis methods, the RBIC
and the RBTB, are evaluated for a large scale structure, in this case the IBB.
Firstly, the problems associated with applying initial conditions for a given struc-
tural problem is discussed. This discussion is followed by an assessment of the
performance of the reduced basis methods, when the IBB is analyzed while assum-
ing zero initial displacement of the bridge, i.e. V0 = 0. The performance of the
RBIC and RBTB are compared to the performance of the non-linear Newmark
algorithm.
5.2.1
Initial Condition Problem
The non-linear dynamic analysis of the IBB should be conducted for the bridge in
the prestressed state, where the permanent loads are included, in order to represent
the stiﬀness and mass of the bridge accurately.
As discussed in section 3.6.1, the prestressing of the main cable in the IBB is gov-
erned by the horizontal dofs in each node, apart from the center node. Furthermore,
the prestressing is performed iteratively with a certain tolerance, which yields a
vertical deﬂection of the main cable and bridge deck girder for the prestressed
state, see Fig. 3.16. The deformed bridge geometry must be included in the non-
linear dynamic analysis as an initial condition, in order to ensure equilibrium in
the model.
This is in conﬂict with the assumptions made in the theory derived for the re-
duced basis methods. In order to simplify the displacement derivatives (4.60) and
the governing equations of modal derivatives (4.62), an assumption of zero initial
geometry is made: V0 = 0. Thus, the displacement derivatives and governing
equations of modal derivatives simpliﬁes to (4.64) and (4.63) respectively.
As the prestressed state of the bridge does not result in a state of zero initial
displacement, the assumption does not apply. Therefore, the equations must be
evaluated without the simplifying assumption of V0 = 0. The consequences of
this are rather comprehensive, as the theory derived for the reduced basis methods
must be reconsidered. The evolution of the theory cannot be ﬁtted inside the time
frame of the of the work surrounding the present report.

==PAGE:131==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
5.2.2
Performance of Reduced Basis Methods for IBB Analysis
In the following section the performance of the reduced basis methods, The RBIC
and the RBTB, are considered for the IBB. As discussed in section 5.2.1, initial
conditions cannot be applied in the basis reduction methods. Therefore, it is impor-
tant to secure that the assumption of V0 = 0 applies. The assumption is satisﬁed
by omitting the prestressing forces, permanent loading and initial geometry from
the model. This, of course, alters the characteristics of the bridge in the model,
as the absence of prestressing results in a less stiﬀstructure. Thus, the natural
frequencies and mode shapes for the bridge determined in section 5.1.4 does not
apply for the considered case. The natural frequencies associated with the ﬁrst six
linear modes are presented table 5.5, and the associated mode shapes are presented
in Appendix P.

When the non-linear Newmark algorithm is utilized, the stability must be fulﬁlled
for all natural frequencies in the discretized bridge meaning, that the eﬀects for
all 1020 modes are included in this analysis. Therefore, the computational time
should be reduced by utilizing the RBIC, as insigniﬁcant modes can be disregarded.
However, when N = 75 linear modes are included in the reduced basis, and the
total number of modal derivatives included in the basis is 1/2 (N + N2) = 2850.

non-linear character and the solution converges at a slower rate. Thus, it is noted
that the solution has not yet converged when N = 100 linear modes are included
in the reduced basis, (
).
For both the load cases considered, the bridge is loaded in order to provoke a
displacement in compliance with linear mode shapes. Therefore, it was expected,
that the displacement could be described reasonably using a limited number of
linear mode shapes in the reduced bases. This was not the case, and the and the
beneﬁts associated with using the RBIC remains absent.

presented in Fig. 5.11.
Consequently, using the RBTB will not result in a solution that converges towards
the correct response, as the second-order Taylor series expansion seems unable to
describe the displacement of the IBB, which was observed from Fig. 5.8 and 5.9.
Attention should be drawn to the fact, that the second-order Taylor series ex-
pansion is evaluated for the point of origin of a given structure, [24]. Thus, the
displacement determined based on the Taylor series becomes increasingly inaccu-
rate as the structure moves away from its origin, i.e. for displacements of great
115
==PAGE:138==

magnitude the second order Taylor series is unable to describe the displacement.

the coordinates to the modal derivatives are determined using the RBIC-method
as in Fig. 5.13.

expansion is considered to be unﬁt for describing the displacement of structures
where displacement beyond moderate magnitudes occurs. Whether the magnitude
of the displacement of a given structure is beyond moderate must be evaluated
for the speciﬁc case considered, as no general rules have been observed. From the
conducted analysis it cannot be concluded ultimately if the error occurring does,
in fact, arise from the truncation error in the Taylor series expansion, and further
and more thorough analyses should be conducted to verify this.

The reduced basis methods, RBIC and RBTB, cannot be utilized in the contin-
ued analysis of the prestressed IBB as further evaluation and development of these
methods must be conducted for them to apply. Consequently, the non-linear New-
mark algorithm is used for further analysis of the prestressed bridge.
5.3
Application Field for Linear and Non-linear Com-
putational Methods in IBB
In the following sections, the application ﬁeld for the linear analysis of the IBB
is established. Initially, a preliminary analysis is conducted, where the amplitude
dependency of the natural frequencies of the IBB is considered. This is followed by
an analysis in which the linear and non-linear response of the IBB are compared,
in order to establish a limit for the linear response in terms of amplitude and
conﬁguration of the oscillation of the bridge.
As the reduced basis methods, RBIC and RBTB, cannot be utilized for this anal-
ysis, the non-linear Newmark algorithm is utilized. For the linear analysis linear
modal analysis is applied.
5.3.1
Preliminary Evaluation of Natural Frequency Variation with
Amplitude in IBB
In the present section, the change in natural frequency in the IBB with respect to
the change in amplitude is evaluated. The evaluation serves as a preliminary anal-
ysis of in which mode shapes the non-linearity of the structure is more pronounced
and is used to assess which load conﬁgurations should be considered in the further
analysis.
The analysis is conducted by applying the mode shape as an initial geometry of
the structure V0 = α ϕi/ϕi,max. The initial deﬂection is scaled by a factor α, and
the stiﬀness is updated according to the deﬂected state. Finally, the generalized
eigenvalue problem is solved:
 KT (V0 + α ϕi/ϕi,max) −ω2 M

ϕ(V) = 0
(5.7)
The natural frequency of the structure is determined from the generalized eigen-
value problem and plotted over the maximum deﬂection uy,max(t) in of the bridge
for the considered deﬂected case. The change in frequency of the IBB with ampli-
tude is depicted for the deﬂected shapes coinciding with the ﬁrst six modes in Fig.
5.14.

For the further analysis it is chosen to consider the loads corresponding to the
deﬂected shape of mode one, as the frequency seems to be rather constant for this
deﬂected shape, mode two as the frequency here changes greatly and asymmetri-
cally, and mode six, as the frequency here changes approximately symmetrically
and quite rapidly. One should keep in mind that the conﬁguration of the deﬂected
state does not change for this analysis. This would probably not be the case for a
full non-linear analysis, as the displacement of the structures would not be bound
to the linear mode shapes.
5.3.2
Limitation of Linear Dynamic Analysis of IBB
In order to evaluate the limitations of the linear analysis, a load scenario must be
considered, where the geometrical non-linearity of the structure becomes essential
for the response. The response of the bridge is determined for three diﬀerent load
scenarios, in compliance with the mode shape, as presented in Fig. 5.1. Based
on the initial frequency dependencies, mode one, two and six are chosen for the
further analysis. Thus, the following three load scenarios are considered:
pϕ1(t)

for the response amplitude to be approximately similar for the considered time

It should be noted that the load only act on the girder. Thus, the load do not fully
coincide with the considered mode shapes, and several modes may be excited by
the considered load. This can be evaluated by considering the modal energy of the
linear response, as calculated using (4.32), and presented in Fig. 5.16.
(a)
(b)
(c)
Fig. 5.16: Mechanical energy in vibration.
It is noted that the majority of the mechanical energy of the system is allocated
in the modes that are deliberately excited. Hence, only an insigniﬁcant amount
of energy is allocated outside the considered modes. For load scenarios where the
bridge is excited by pϕ2 and pϕ6, no visible energy is detected in the other modes.
Contrarily, it is noted that when the bridge is excited by pϕ1, a small amount of
energy is contained in mode shape three. Hence, the excitation by pϕ1 results in a
small excitation of mode three.
For the following analyses, it is highly relevant to keep in mind that the load
frequency remains constant. As the natural frequency of the structure changes
when the geometrical non-linearity begins to aﬀect the response, the structure will
be loaded out of phase. In an actual case, the shedding of vortices is governed by
the oscillation of the bridge and the load would remain in phase even for a non-
linear response. The fact that the load frequency remains constant might cause
the conﬁguration of the oscillation to deviate from what the actual conﬁguration
would be. Furthermore, it is possible that the amplitude of the non-linear response
119
==PAGE:142==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
would increase further than seen in the following analysis if the load frequency was
bound to the bridge oscillation.

Considering Fig. 5.18(a) it is noted that the non-linear character of the response in
location (C) becomes increasingly more pronounced with the increase in amplitude,
and starts deviating from the linear response at amplitudes of approximately 8 m
as depicted in Fig. Q.2. Furthermore, it is noted that the downward motion is
more restrained than the upward motion for the non-linear response, which can be
explained the increase in the stiﬀness of the cable caused by the downward motion.
Attention should be drawn to the fact that the natural frequency of the structure
changes with the changing stiﬀness meaning, that load frequency does not coincide
with the natural frequency of the structure, i.e. ωe ≈ωnon.lin. This results in
the non-linear response deviating further from the linear response, as the linear

==PAGE:143==

response continues to increase while the non-linear response obtains a steady-state
after an initial transient response as seen in Appendix Q.

starts deviating from the linear response at an amplitude of approximately 1 m, as
depicted in Q.2. Furthermore, it is noted that the non-linear response changes in
frequency at relatively low amplitudes, and oscillates at approximately twice the
rate of the linear response. This could be due to the period-doubling bifurcation
discussed in section 4.6. Furthermore, the non-linear response in the side spans
reaches amplitudes of signiﬁcantly higher magnitude than the linear response. In
this context, it should be noted, that the linear response does not seem to resonate,
which can be explained by the load, which does not coincide perfectly with the
mode shape.

Similarly to what was observed for the bridge loaded by pϕ1(t), it is observed from
Fig. 5.21(a) that the non-linearity of the response in location (C) becomes in-
creasingly more pronounced with the increase in amplitude, and starts deviating
from the linear response at amplitudes of approximately 11 m, as depicted in Fig.
Q.4. In the present case, it is noted that the downward motion of the non-linear
response is restrained when comparing to the linear response. This phenomenon
can once again be described by considering the change in stiﬀness of the cable as-
sociated with the geometrical non-linearity, as the stiﬀness of the cable is increased
for downward motion. For the upward motion of the bridge deck, it is observed
122
==PAGE:145==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
that the amplitude of the non-linear response exceeds the amplitude of the linear
response immediately after the response starts deviating. This can be explained by
the decrease of the cable stiﬀness for the upward motion. For the continued oscil-
lation it is observed that the maxima for the non-linear response stabilize, whereas
the maxima for linear response increases continuously. This can be explained by
the change in natural frequency of the structure, which disturbs the resonance of
the non-linear response causing the aforementioned behavior where a somewhat
steady state is obtained after an initial transient response as seen in Appendix Q.
Considering Fig. 5.21(b) it is noted that the non-linear response of the side span
at location (D) behaves quite similarly to that of the main span, but with oppo-
site sign, as would be expected based on the linear mode shapes. This behavior
coincides well with the amplitude dependency of the frequency, which is depicted
in Fig. 5.22.

Fig. 5.25: Change in natural frequency for the bridge loaded by pϕ6(t)
plotted over (a) time and (b) maximum vertical deﬂection.
From Fig. 5.25(a) it is observed that the frequency initially changes at approxi-
mately twice the rate of the oscillation depicted in Fig. 5.24(a). As time elapse the
rate of change for the frequency increases, and the period between to local maxima
is reduced to 1/4 of the period of the response T6. Considering Fig. 5.25(a) it
seems as if the natural frequency of the bridge deviates from the linear natural
frequency ω6 and ﬁnds equilibrium at approximately 0.9 ω6 from which it develops.
This coincides with the behavior seen in 5.25(b), where it is observed that the de-
velopment in frequency with amplitude considered in Fig. 5.14 overestimates the
mean frequency of the structure. Once again misinterpretation of the frequency
development depicted in Fig. 5.14 can be explained by the great change in the
conﬁguration of the bridge oscillation that occurs for the non-linear response. In
Fig. 5.25(b) a great spread of the natural frequencies of the bridge associated with
the deﬂection in (D) is observed. Comparing to Fig. 5.19(b), the spread even seems
to increase as the response of the bridge becomes increasingly more chaotic. This
can be explained by the conﬁguration of the bridge during the oscillation deviates
signiﬁcantly from the linear mode shape.
As the IBB is a rather large structure it can be diﬃcult to assess if the displacements
observed in Fig. 5.18, Fig. 5.21 and Fig. 5.24 are of reasonable magnitude or if
they are unfeasible to obtain. Therefore, it is evaluated if the displacements of the
bridge result in unrealistically high stress levels in the structural members, leading
to yielding and failure of the bridge.
The stress analysis is conducted similarly to the static stress analysis in section
3.6.2. Thus, the normal stress is calculated by applying a linear Navier stress dis-
tribution throughout the cross-section, the shear stress is determined by Grashof’s

Based on the evaluation of the utilization of the structural members performed
for the diﬀerent load scenarios presented in 5.15, it can be concluded that the
conﬁguration of the bridge oscillation for the non-linear response begins to devi-
ate from the conﬁguration of the bridge oscillation for the linear response before
131
==PAGE:154==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
yielding failure of the bridge occurs. It should be noted, that the stress analysis
is performed without taking local instability eﬀects of the structural members into
account. Thus, the bridge would possibly experience failure before yielding of the
structural elements.
As seen from the animations presented in 05-Animation on the appended USB the
non-linear oscillation of the bridge is rather complex, and it is quite cumbersome
to describe. Furthermore, it can be diﬃcult to evaluate at which amplitudes the
non-linearity begins to inﬂuence the response signiﬁcantly. In Appendix Q, various
plots have been depicted to clarify at which amplitudes the non-linearities in the
structure should be accounted for.
In general, it seems as if the oscillation of
the side spans is inﬂuenced by the non-linearity of the structure for rather small
amplitudes compared to the main spans, for the load scenarios exciting the anti-
symmetrical linear mode shapes, Fig. 5.15(a) and Fig. 5.15(c). Whereas, the non-
linearities aﬀect the main- and side span similarly for the load scenario exciting
the symmetrical linear mode shape, Fig. 5.15(b).
The non-linear eﬀect observed in the side spans must oﬀcourse be considered
while keeping in mind, that the great displacement in the main span could force
the response in the side span to become non-linear. Thus, the greatest displacement
of the bridge deck at the point in time, where the non-linear response of part of
the bridge deviates signiﬁcantly from the linear response of set part, is used as
a threshold for when the bridge should be considered using a non-linear analysis.
This point is observed at amplitudes of approximately: 8 m displacement in (C), for
load scenario 5.15(a), and 11 m displacement in (D), for load scenario 5.15(b) and 2
m displacement in (D), for load scenario 5.15(c). Consequently, the non-linearity of
the bridge vibration is observed to depend highly on the shape of the oscillation, as
the non-linear character of the oscillation is governed by the derivative of transverse
motion, i.e. the inclination of the response, as presented in the Lagrangian strain
measure (2.5).
According to [31] diﬀerent measures has been made for the Great Belt East bridge,
where natural frequencies of 0.13-0.21 Hz and a maximum response amplitude of
320 mm were observed for the phenomena of vortex shedding. Based on this inves-
tigation it is assessed, that the non-linear eﬀects in the bridge can be disregarded
for vortex shedding, due to the low amplitudes observed for this phenomenon. It
can however not be concluded, that the geometrical non-linearity of the bridge
should not be accounted for when phenomena as ﬂutter, buﬀeting and earthquakes
are analyzed. This requires further analysis of the amplitudes that can be obtained
from such load scenarios.
132
==PAGE:155==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
5.4
Conclusion
In Chapter 2 the theoretical basis for geometrically non-linear static FE-analysis
was established. The implementation of the non-linear Lagrangian strain measure
in the FEM was discussed for a plane beam element, and the theory was evolved
to account for prestressing of the elements.
Furthermore, the establishment of
the nodal force vector and the tangential stiﬀness matrix was discussed, while
taking into account the contributions coming from the non-linear strain measure
and prestressing force in the element.
The applied theory was implemented in the locally based FE-program BygFEM.
The implementation of the Lagrangian strain measure in the nodal force vector
and tangential stiﬀness matrix was veriﬁed by comparing the load-displacement
curve of a simple angle frame to known solutions.
The implementation of the
prestressing force was checked by considering a single element cantilevered beam
for six diﬀerent load scenarios. By comparing the load scenarios it was concluded,
that the implementation of the prestressing force was concluded to be correct.
In Chapter 3 the IBB was considered, and a plane beam model was established in
BygFEM.
In order to suﬃciently represent the properties of the IBB, the deﬂection arising
from the permanent load of the bridge should be accounted for, and a prestressing
algorithm was established for this purpose. Utilizing the algorithm, a layout for
the prestressing of the main cable and hangers was determined and accepted for
further analysis as the criteria for displacement of the nodes in the main cable
(ux, uy,mid) ≤5 cm and the bridge deck girder uy ≤5 cm were fulﬁlled. However,
a vertical displacement of up to 1.5 m was observed for the main cable.
After obtaining a satisfying prestressed state for the bridge, static analyses were
conducted for diﬀerent conﬁgurations of traﬃc load, and the critical states of de-
ﬂection and stress were evaluated. The maximum displacement of the bridge was
determined as L/225 = 7.0 m for a case with traﬃc load on the main span. The
maximum stress determined in the bridge yielded a ratio of utilization of ≤57%.
It should be kept in mind, however, that the analyses were conducted without con-
sidering limit states. The deﬂections and stress states were concluded to be within
an acceptable limit.
In Chapter 4 the theoretical basis for linear and geometrically non-linear dynamic
FE-analysis was established.
The theory surrounding the non-linear Newmark
algorithm was discussed, and the non-linear Newmark algorithm was implemented
in BygFEM. The algorithm was tested for an analysis performed on a simple taut
cable, and stability was obtained at time steps as high as TJ/7. The algorithm
provided with accurate results for time steps of TJ/40. It was, however, concluded
that a convergence analysis should always be conducted in order to secure accurate
results.
Furthermore, the theory surrounding the reduced basis methods, RBIC and RBTB,
133
==PAGE:156==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
was established.
The RBIC-method and RBTB-method were implemented in
BygFEM and tested for analyses performed on a simple taut cable. It was con-
cluded, that the modal derivatives associated with the ﬁrst three linear modes
were determined correctly for the considered case. Additionally, it was concluded
that the modal coordinates associated with the ﬁrst three linear modes fulﬁlled
the second-order Taylor series expansion of the displacement (4.71). Thus, the
implementation of the reduced basis methods in BygFEM was concluded to be
correct.
The stability and numerical accuracy of the reduced basis methods were evaluated
in terms of time step size ∆t and number of linear modes and associated modal
derivatives that should be included in the reduced bases.
For both reduced basis methods it was concluded, that stability was ensured for
all considered time step sizes, even for time steps yielding meaningless results in
terms of accuracy. Hence, stability was not considered further. The reduced basis
methods both provided accurate results for time steps of TJ/40 and three linear
mode shapes and associated modal derivatives included in the reduced bases. It
was, however, concluded that the number of linear mode shapes that should be
included in the bases, should be evaluated for the speciﬁc load case, as the accuracy
depends highly on the exciting load. Finally, it was concluded, that a convergence
analysis should always be conducted in terms of time step size in order to ensure
accurate results.
The non-linear CDM, the non-linear Newmark algorithm, the RBIC-method and
the RBTB-method was evaluated in terms of computational eﬃciency for an anal-
ysis performed on a simple taut cable. It was concluded, that a time step size of
∆t = 1.1 10−4 s should be used for the CDM to ensure stability, while the three
latter methods could do with a time step size of ∆t = 0.2 s. Thus the CDM was
concluded to be highly ineﬃcient compared to the other methods for the considered
case. Furthermore, it was concluded that both the RBIC and RBTB performed
more eﬃciently than the non-linear Newmark algorithm, due to the reduced num-
ber of governing equations to be solved. Additionally, the increased eﬃciency of
the reduced basis methods became even more pronounced when the number of
dofs in the taut cable was increased. Thus, it was concluded that a potentially
great reduction of CPU-time could be observed when utilizing the reduced basis
methods for comprehensive structures. Finally, as opposed to what was expected,
the RBIC-method performed more eﬃciently than the RBTB-method. This was
concluded to be due to the high stability of the Newmark algorithm used as the
numerical solver for the modal coordinates. Thus, the beneﬁts of introducing the
additional transformation in RBTB remained absent.
In the ﬁrst part of Chapter 5 the IBB is considered in order to evaluate the perfor-
mance of the reduced basis methods. Here an assumption of zero initial displace-
ment of the bridge is applied, as it is concluded by considering the theory derived
for the reduced basis methods, that initial displacements cannot be accounted for.
For the RBIC-method an unfavorably low convergence rate was observed and for
134
==PAGE:157==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
the RBTB-method the solution to the response for the bridge was observed to
converge towards an incorrect solution. This lead to an investigation of the modal
coordinates and coordinates for the modal derivatives. It was concluded that the
modal coordinates and derived modal coordinates associated with the ﬁrst three
linear modes of the bridge did not fulﬁll the second-order Taylor series expansion
of the displacement (4.71).
Based on these investigations, and further investigation of a simple taut cable
subjected to displacements of great magnitude, it was concluded, that the second-
order Taylor series expansion of the displacement ﬁeld is unﬁt for describing the
displacement of structures where displacements beyond moderate magnitudes oc-
cur, possibly due to a truncation error caused by the great displacements.
In the second part of Chapter 5 the application ﬁeld of linear and non-linear dy-
namic analysis was evaluated for the IBB. It was concluded, that the non-linearity
of the displacement increased for an increasing amplitude resulting in more com-
plex conﬁgurations of the oscillation, before yielding of the structural members was
experienced. Furthermore, it was concluded, that the non-linearity of the dynamic
response was highly inﬂuenced by the conﬁguration and frequency of the exciting
load, yielding the shape of the oscillation. Finally, it was concluded, that non-
linear eﬀects can be disregarded when considering vortex shedding, as documented
measurements have shown, that the amplitudes do not usually increase to a critical
level. However, it could not be concluded that the non-linearity of the structure
should not be considered when e.g. ﬂutter, buﬀeting or earthquakes are analyzed.
5.4.1
Further Work
The present work formulates a number of topics of certain interest for further
work concerning the matter of kinematic non-linear modeling of structures. The
reduced basis methods proved potentially eﬃcient concerning the computational
time for simple kinematic non-linear structure. However, when the basis reduction
methods were applied for the suspension bridge a number of problems occurred.
Furthermore, the non-linear modeling of suspension bridges could be reﬁned in
order to establish a more precise application ﬁeld for the linear analysis.
• Initial Condition Problem: The considered reduced basis method applied
the assumption of zero initial deﬂection ﬁeld V0 = 0 in derived theory. This
limits the possibility of applying an initial geometry in the analysis. Thus,
it is found interesting to evaluate the reduced basis for an initial geometry,
hence seek a formulation where this could be applied.
• Taylor Series Expansion of Displacement Field: For structures with de-
formation of relatively great amplitudes, the solution seems to deviate due to
a truncation error in the second-order Taylor series expansion of the displace-
ment ﬁeld. Thus, the formulation of the reduced basis in the present form
is concluded to be inapplicable for the suspension bridge. Consequently, it is
135
==PAGE:158==
Chapter 5.
Case Study for Evaluation of Non-linear Dynamics
found relevant to evaluate and establish an application ﬁeld for the present
second-order expansion or develop the theory for a higher order expansion.
• Load update: In the non-linear analysis, the natural frequency of the struc-
ture changes as the deﬂection ﬁeld changes. In the FE-program BygFEM,
the phase in with the dynamic load excites the structure is kept constant.
Thus, the structure will be loaded out of phase when the geometrical non-
linearity begins to aﬀect the response.
In an actual case the shedding of
vortices, a structure-wind-relations applies such that the oscillation of the
bridge and the load tends to remain in phase even for a non-linear response.
The fact that the load frequency remains constant might cause the conﬁgu-
ration of the oscillation to deviate from what the actual conﬁguration would
be. Thus, it would be a subject of interest to implement the dynamic load as
f(t, ωi(V)), to account for the dependency between the load and structural
frequency.
• Numerical model: In the present work, the suspension bridge calculations
are conducted on a plane beam model with a resonant load in compliance
with the linear mode shape applied. Thus, it is found relevant to expand
the numerical model to 3 dimensions, in order to evaluate the eﬀects of e.g.
torsion, ﬂutter instabilities etc. Furthermore, additional dynamic load situa-
tions could be applied, e.g. seismic actions etc.

Appendix C. Strain Interpolation Mismatch and Roorda’s Frame
The axial Lagrange strain measure ε, (2.18), consist of two terms. The ﬁrst term,
Bεvl is a polynomial of the ﬁrst order, as Bε is the ﬁrst row of the strain inter-
polation matrix B. The second term, 1/2 (vl)T Gvl, however, is a polynomial of
the fourth order. This mismatch in polynomial degree can result in locking of the
element, cf. [11], which results in poor performance of the element, requiring a
great number of elements to reach converged solutions. Diﬀerent solutions have
been proposed to overcome this mismatch, cf. [11]. In the present work, a simple
solution is to apply a G-matrix averaged over the length of the element.
In the following section, it is assessed if the implementation of an average G-
matrix, referred to as G, can improve convergence in the solution by eliminating
the mismatch between the polynomial order in the two terms describing the non-
linear strain measure for the axial strain.
The average G-matrix, G, can be expressed as:
G = 1
L
Z L
0
G dxl
(C.1)
and has been determined in Appendix B. Conveniently, G can simply be imple-
mented in stead of G for the beam formulation, which is described in section 2.4,
2.5 and 2.6. In order to assess if the implementation of G beneﬁcial eﬀect on the
rate of convergence a classic problem, namely Roorda’s frame, is considered.
Roorda’s frame is depicted in Fig. C.1. It is modeled using the Lagrange beam
element. The beams are chosen with a length of L = 5 m, and a cross section
corresponding to HE300B steel proﬁles. The frame is loaded by a vertical load P
and a moment M = e P, with an eccentricity of e = L/500, resulting in a negative
rotation of the frame edge.

% Algorithm check for the nodal force vector
function check
clear all
clc
% %%%%%% EKSEMPEL/TEST %%%%%%
EA = 100;
L  = 4;
EI = 100;
D = zeros(2);
D(1,1) = EA; D(2,2) = EI;
% Displacement field
% Rigid body translation
ve_1 = [1 0 0 1 0 0]';
ve_2 = [0 1 0 0 1 0]';
% Rigid body rotation
y     = 0.01;
x     = L/2 - sqrt((L/2)^2-y^2);
Lx    = L-2*x;
theta = y/sqrt((L/2)^2-y^2);
ve_3 = [x y -theta -x -y -theta]';
ve_mat = [ve_1 ve_2 ve_3];
for i= 1:3
ve   = ve_mat(:,i);
% integration points and weights
nip = 3;
zp = sqrt(3/5);
zip = [1-zp 1 1+zp]/2;
wip = [5/9 8/9 5/9]/2;
k = zeros(6);
for ip=1:nip
  z = zip(ip);
  w = wip(ip);
  % strain interpolation matrix
  B = Bbeam(L,z);
  Be= B(1,:);
  % Geometric matrix
  G = beam2D_G(z,L);
  % Constitutive stiffness
  kc= B'*D*B;
  % Geometric stiffness
  kg=(EA/2)*(Be'*ve'*G+2*G*ve*Be+G*ve*ve'*G);
  % Element siffness
  k = k+w*L*(kc+kg);
end

% function kp = kpbeam(L,el,sys)
%   kp = zeros(6,6);
%   kp(2,2) =  -sys.P(el)/L;
%   kp(2,5) =   sys.P(el)/L;
%   kp(5,2) =   sys.P(el)/L;
%   kp(5,5) =  -sys.P(el)/L;

function B = Bbeam(L,z)
  B = zeros(2,6);
  B(1,1) = -1/L;
  B(1,4) =  1/L;
  B(2,2) = -(6-12*z)/L^2;
  B(2,3) = (-4+6*z)/L;
  B(2,5) = (6-12*z)/L^2;
  B(2,6) = (-2+6*z)/L;
end
end

% Algorithm check for the tangential stiffness matrix
function check
clear all
clc
% %%%%%% EKSEMPEL/TEST %%%%%%
EA = 100;
L  = 4;
EI = 100;
% Displacement field
% Rigid body translation
ve_1 = [1 0 0 1 0 0]';
ve_2 = [0 1 0 0 1 0]';
% Rigid body rotation
y     = 0.01;
x     = L/2 - sqrt((L/2)^2-y^2);
Lx    = L-2*x;
theta = y/sqrt((L/2)^2-y^2);
ve_3 = [x y -theta -x -y -theta]';
ve_mat = [ve_1 ve_2 ve_3];
for i= 1:3
ve   = ve_mat(:,i);
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
% integration points and weights
nip = 3;
zp = sqrt(3/5);
zip = [1-zp 1 1+zp]/2;
wip = [5/9 8/9 5/9]/2;
D = [EA 0; 0 EI];                      % material stiffness matrix
I1 = [1;0];
k = zeros(6);
for ip=1:nip
  z = zip(ip);
  w = wip(ip);
  B = Bbeam(L,z);
  %B = ElTy.B(x,ElTy.zrp(ip))
  G = beam2D_G(z,L);
  %G = ElTy.G(ElTy.zrp(ip),L)
  % Lagrangian strain meassure
  e1 = B*ve+1/2*I1*(ve'*G*ve);
  % stress (normal force)
  s = EA*e1(1);
  % make k_sigma for local directions
  k = k+w*L*s*G;
  % make k_0 for local directions
  B1 = B+I1*(ve'*G);
1

  k = k+w*L*B1'*D*B1;
end
% %%%% TEST %%%%
 Qeq = k*ve
% %%%%%%%%%%%%%%
end

  B = zeros(2,6);
  B(1,1) = -1/L;

  B(2,2) = -(6-12*z)/L^2;
  B(2,3) = (-4+6*z)/L;
  B(2,5) = (6-12*z)/L^2;
  B(2,6) = (-2+6*z)/L;
end

The main cable curve follows a parabola, when sublected to uniform dead load
